#01

Linearly Separable Dichotomies of a Neural Code (Cover Counting)

📄 Submission snapshot — parsed source evidence (private bank, unreleased; do not redistribute)
A downstream readout neuron assigns a binary label to each of its presynaptic activity patterns by means of a linear threshold with \emph{no bias termstrong: Explicitly flagging the homogeneous convention up front avoids the affine-vs-homogeneous ambiguity that's the whole trap.} (a hyperplane through the origin). Concretely, let $x^{(1)},\dots,x^{(P)} \in \mathbb{R}^{N}$ be $P$ activity patterns in \emph{general position} (every subset of size $\le N$ is linearly independentstrong: Good — defines 'general position' inline rather than assuming the reader's convention matches yours.). A \emph{dichotomy} is an assignment of labels in $\{+1,-1\}$ to the $P$ patterns; it is \emph{homogeneously linearly separable} if there exists $w \in \mathbb{R}^{N}$ with $\operatorname{sign}(w^{\top} x^{(i)})$ equal to the assigned label for every $i$. Take $P = 12$ patterns in general position in $\mathbb{R}^{4}$. \begin{enumerate} \item How many of the $2^{12}$ possible dichotomies are homogeneously linearly separable? Give the exact integer. \item What fraction of all dichotomies is this? Give the answer as a reduced fraction. \end{enumerate} Report the exact integer and the reduced fraction.

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

Cover's function-counting theorem states that the number of homogeneously linearly separable dichotomies of $P$ points in general position in $\mathbb{R}^{N}$ is \[ C(P,N) = 2\sum_{k=0}^{N-1}\binom{P-1}{k}. \] For $P=12$, $N=4$: \[ C(12,4) = 2\left[\binom{11}{0}+\binom{11}{1}+\binom{11}{2}+\binom{11}{3}\right] = 2\,[\,1 + 11 + 55 + 165\,] = 2\cdot 232 = \boxed{464}. \] The fraction is \[ \frac{464}{2^{12}} = \frac{464}{4096} = \boxed{\dfrac{29}{256}} \approx 0.11328. \] (Sanity check: $P=12 > 2N = 8$, so we are past the storage-capacity point $P=2N$ where exactly half of all dichotomies are separable; the fraction $29/256 \approx 0.113 < 1/2$ is consistent with this regime.)

Rubric (grading scheme) — how a verifier is meant to grade an answer.

\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: count $= 464$; fraction $= 29/256$. \item Numerical tolerance: none for the integer; the fraction must be exactly $29/256$ (equivalently $0.11328125$). \item Acceptable equivalent forms: $29/256$, $0.11328125$, or $464/4096$ explicitly identified as reducible to $29/256$. \item Deterministic verifier: compare the integer to $464$ and the fraction to $\mathrm{Fraction}(29,256)$. See \path{contributors/contributor_02/_verify.py}. \end{itemize} A response that gives the count via the affine formula $\big(2\sum_{k=0}^{N}\binom{P-1}{k}\big)$, or that sums to $k=N$, or that omits the leading factor $2$, is incorrect.

\problemsetup
 {Linearly Separable Dichotomies of a Neural Code (Cover Counting)}
 {Mathematics / Statistics}
 {Combinatorial geometry / neural coding capacity}
 {Qualifying-exam-plus}
 {Contributor 02 (Dongyu Gong)}
 {\href{mailto:dongyu.gong@yale.edu}{dongyu.gong@yale.edu}}
 {Ready for review}
\problemtags
 {compute}
 {combinatorial reasoning, exact recall vs.\ rederivation of a counting formula}
 {exact match (integer and reduced fraction)}
 {off-by-one in the binomial upper limit, dropped factor of 2, or affine-vs-homogeneous confusion}
\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
A downstream readout neuron assigns a binary label to each of its presynaptic activity patterns by means of a linear threshold with \emph{no bias term} (a hyperplane through the origin). Concretely, let $x^{(1)},\dots,x^{(P)} \in \mathbb{R}^{N}$ be $P$ activity patterns in \emph{general position} (every subset of size $\le N$ is linearly independent). A \emph{dichotomy} is an assignment of labels in $\{+1,-1\}$ to the $P$ patterns; it is \emph{homogeneously linearly separable} if there exists $w \in \mathbb{R}^{N}$ with $\operatorname{sign}(w^{\top} x^{(i)})$ equal to the assigned label for every $i$.

Take $P = 12$ patterns in general position in $\mathbb{R}^{4}$.
\begin{enumerate}
\item How many of the $2^{12}$ possible dichotomies are homogeneously linearly separable? Give the exact integer.
\item What fraction of all dichotomies is this? Give the answer as a reduced fraction.
\end{enumerate}
Report the exact integer and the reduced fraction.
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
Cover's function-counting theorem states that the number of homogeneously linearly separable dichotomies of $P$ points in general position in $\mathbb{R}^{N}$ is
\[
C(P,N) = 2\sum_{k=0}^{N-1}\binom{P-1}{k}.
\]
For $P=12$, $N=4$:
\[
C(12,4) = 2\left[\binom{11}{0}+\binom{11}{1}+\binom{11}{2}+\binom{11}{3}\right]
 = 2\,[\,1 + 11 + 55 + 165\,] = 2\cdot 232 = \boxed{464}.
\]
The fraction is
\[
\frac{464}{2^{12}} = \frac{464}{4096} = \boxed{\dfrac{29}{256}} \approx 0.11328.
\]
(Sanity check: $P=12 > 2N = 8$, so we are past the storage-capacity point $P=2N$ where exactly half of all dichotomies are separable; the fraction $29/256 \approx 0.113 < 1/2$ is consistent with this regime.)
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: count $= 464$; fraction $= 29/256$.
\item Numerical tolerance: none for the integer; the fraction must be exactly $29/256$ (equivalently $0.11328125$).
\item Acceptable equivalent forms: $29/256$, $0.11328125$, or $464/4096$ explicitly identified as reducible to $29/256$.
\item Deterministic verifier: compare the integer to $464$ and the fraction to $\mathrm{Fraction}(29,256)$. See \path{contributors/contributor_02/_verify.py}.
\end{itemize}
A response that gives the count via the affine formula $\big(2\sum_{k=0}^{N}\binom{P-1}{k}\big)$, or that sums to $k=N$, or that omits the leading factor $2$, is incorrect.
\end{gradingscheme}

\begin{editornotes}
The trap is recall fidelity of a famous formula: the binomial sum runs to $N-1$ (not $N$), the leading factor is $2$, and the ``no bias'' (homogeneous) convention is what makes the upper limit $N-1$ rather than $N$. The arithmetic is trivial once the formula is exactly right, so this isolates formula-memory failures from computation.
\end{editornotes}

\end{msbproblem}

\end{document}

## Problem P = 12 patterns in general position in R^4. A dichotomy is homogeneously linearly separable if a weight vector w (no bias) exists with sign(w^T x^(i)) matching the assigned label for all i. (1) How many of the 2^12 dichotomies are homogeneously linearly separable? (2) What fraction of all dichotomies is this? --- ## Approach: Cover's Function-Counting Theorem (Homogeneous Version) Cover's theorem for HOMOGENEOUS linear separability (hyperplane through the origin, no bias term) states: C(P, N) = 2 * sum_{k=0}^{N-1} C(P-1, k) Key detail: the upper limit is N-1 (not N). This is because the "no bias" constraint reduces the effective VC dimension from N+1 (affine) to N (homogeneous). When a bias is allowed, the formula sums to k=N; without bias it sums only to k=N-1. --- ## Part 1: Count of Linearly Separable Dichotomies With P = 12, N = 4: C(12, 4) = 2 * [C(11,0) + C(11,1) + C(11,2) + C(11,3)] Computing each binomial coefficient: - C(11,0) = 1 - C(11,1) = 11 - C(11,2) = 11*10 / 2! = 110/2 = 55 - C(11,3) = 11*10*9 / 3! = 990/6 = 165 Sum = 1 + 11 + 55 + 165 = 232 C(12, 4) = 2 * 232 = 464 **Answer to Part 1: 464** --- ## Part 2: Fraction of All Dichotomies Total number of dichotomies = 2^12 = 4096 Fraction = 464 / 4096 To reduce: find GCD(464, 4096). - 464 = 2^4 * 29 = 16 * 29 - 4096 = 2^12 = 16 * 256 GCD = 16, so: 464/4096 = 29/256 **Answer to Part 2: 29/256** --- ## Sanity Check P = 12 > 2N = 8, so we are past the storage capacity point. At exactly P = 2N, half of all dichotomies are separable. Beyond this threshold, the fraction drops below 1/2. Our answer 29/256 ≈ 0.1133 < 0.5 is consistent with being in the super-capacity regime. --- ## Final Answers 1. **464** (exact integer count of homogeneously linearly separable dichotomies) 2. **29/256** (reduced fraction of all 2^12 = 4096 dichotomies)

Using Cover's function-counting theorem, the number of homogeneously linearly separable dichotomies of P points in general position in R^N is C(P,N) = 2·Σ_{k=0}^{N-1} C(P-1,k). For P=12, N=4: 2·(C(11,0)+C(11,1)+C(11,2)+C(11,3)) = 2·(1+11+55+165) = 2·232 = 464. (1) Exactly 464 dichotomies are homogeneously linearly separable. (2) Fraction of all 2^12 = 4096 dichotomies: 464/4096 = 29/256.

With P=12, d=4: C(12,4) = 2*[C(11,0)+C(11,1)+C(11,2)+C(11,3)] = 2*[1+11+55+165] = 2*232 = 464. (2) Fraction = 464 / 2^12 = 464/4096 = 29/256.

free-subagent k0Count = 464; fraction = 29/256 ✓ pass0/0—
trace
response
**Approach.** This is a direct application of Cover's function-counting theorem. For $P$ points in general position in $\mathbb{R}^N$, the number of dichotomies separable by a homogeneous hyperplane (linear threshold through the origin, no bias) is $$C(P,N) = 2\sum_{k=0}^{N-1}\binom{P-1}{k}.$$ The "no bias" (homogeneous) convention is exactly what makes the upper limit $N-1$ rather than $N$; the leading factor 2 comes from the fact that each separable labeling and its complement are both realizable. **Part 1 — exact integer.** With $P=12$, $N=4$: $$C(12,4) = 2\left[\binom{11}{0}+\binom{11}{1}+\binom{11}{2}+\binom{11}{3}\right].$$ Compute the binomials: - $\binom{11}{0}=1$ - $\binom{11}{1}=11$ - $\binom{11}{2}=\frac{11\cdot10}{2}=55$ - $\binom{11}{3}=\frac{11\cdot10\cdot9}{6}=165$ Sum $= 1+11+55+165 = 232$. Then $C(12,4)=2\cdot232 = 464$. **Part 2 — reduced fraction.** Total dichotomies $=2^{12}=4096$, so the fraction separable is $$\frac{464}{4096}.$$ $\gcd(464,4096)$: $464 = 16\cdot29$, $4096 = 16\cdot256$, so the fraction reduces to $$\frac{29}{256} \approx 0.11328125.$$ **Sanity check.** Since $P=12 > 2N=8$, we are past the storage-capacity threshold $P=2N$ (where exactly half of all dichotomies are separable). Hence the fraction should be below $1/2$, and indeed $29/256\approx0.113 < 0.5$, consistent with the over-capacity regime. **Final answer:** count $=464$; fraction $=29/256$.

  • Summary: Hypothesized pattern (to be confirmed against the pasted runs): models recall Cover's theorem but reproduce it with a corrupted upper limit (summing to `k=N` rather than `k=N-1`), drop the leading factor of 2, or silently switch to the affine-separability count. Because the arithmetic is trivial, any error is almost entirely a formula-fidelity failure rather than a computation failure — which is exactly the signal of interest. Likely near-miss: getting the structure right but reporting an unreduced or off-by-one fraction.

Generated Python verifier (176 lines) 
"""Deterministic verifier — contributor_02 / problem_01.
Linearly Separable Dichotomies (Cover Counting). Hand-authored (Claude Code, autonomous; via @yulinl2).
"""

import re
from fractions import Fraction

_MARKERS = [
 r"final\s+answer\s*[:=]?", r"the\s+(?:exact\s+)?answer\s+is\s*[:=]?",
 r"answer\s*[:=]", r"the\s+rate\s+is\s*[:=]?", r"therefore[,:]?",
 r"thus[,:]?", r"hence[,:]?",
]
_MARKER_RE = re.compile("|".join(_MARKERS), re.IGNORECASE)

def _boxed_inner(s):
 """Brace-balanced contents of every \\boxed{...} (handles nested \\frac)."""
 out, i = [], 0
 while True:
 j = s.find("boxed", i)
 if j < 0:
 break
 k = s.find("{", j)
 if k < 0:
 break
 depth, m = 0, k
 while m < len(s):
 if s[m] == "{":
 depth += 1
 elif s[m] == "}":
 depth -= 1
 if depth == 0:
 break
 m += 1
 out.append(s[k + 1:m])
 i = m + 1
 return out

def _normalize(s):
 """LaTeX -> plain so rationals survive: \\frac{a}{b} -> a/b, keep %.
 (c09 fractions are always integer/integer, so the flat a/b form is exact;
 a compound numerator would need parens, not present here.)"""
 # capture an optional leading minus OUTSIDE the frac (-\frac{a}{b} -> -a/b):
 # a detached "-" would otherwise be split off and the component read positive.
 s = re.sub(r"(-?)\s*\\[dt]?frac\s*\{([^{}]*)\}\s*\{([^{}]*)\}", r" \1\2/\3 ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\(left|right|!|,|;|:|quad|qquad)", " ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\text\s*\{[^{}]*\}", " ", s)
 s = s.replace(r"\%", "%").replace(r"\$", " ").replace("$", " ")
 s = s.replace("{,}", "") # 225{,}000 -> 225000
 s = s.replace("\\", " ").replace("{", " ").replace("}", " ")
 # thousands separator ONLY when strictly adjacent (1,250 -> 1250); never join
 # across a space (a vector's "1907, 379" must stay two numbers).
 s = re.sub(r"(\d),(\d{3})(?!\d)", r"\1\2", s)
 return s

# token: percent | fraction a/b | decimal | integer (left-to-right, non-overlap)
_TOK = re.compile(
 r"(?P<pct>-?\d+(?:\.\d+)?)\s*%"
 r"|(?P<n>-?\d+)\s*/\s*(?P<d>-?\d+)"
 r"|(?P<dec>-?\d+\.\d+)"
 r"|(?P<int>-?\d+)")

def _rationals(zone):
 """Ordered list of Fraction values committed in `zone` (already a string)."""
 out = []
 for m in _TOK.finditer(_normalize(zone)):
 try:
 if m.group("pct") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("pct")) / 100)
 elif m.group("n") is not None:
 d = int(m.group("d"))
 if d == 0:
 continue
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("n")), d))
 elif m.group("dec") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("dec")))
 else:
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("int"))))
 except (ValueError, ZeroDivisionError):
 continue
 return out

def _final_zone(response):
 """The text of the model's committed final answer (most -> least reliable):
 boxed contents; else the last marker line's tail; else the last line."""
 if not response or not response.strip:
 return ""
 boxed = _boxed_inner(response)
 if boxed:
 return " ".join(boxed)
 lines = [ln for ln in response.splitlines if ln.strip]
 # last marker line that actually carries a number (weak discourse markers
 # like "therefore/thus/hence" otherwise hijack the zone to a number-less
 # trailing comment, burying the real commit on the line above).
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 m = _MARKER_RE.search(ln)
 if m and any(c.isdigit for c in ln[m.start:]):
 return ln[m.start:]
 # else the last line that carries a number
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 if any(c.isdigit for c in ln):
 return ln
 return lines[-1] if lines else ""

def _committed_rational(zone):
 """The single COMMITTED final rational in `zone`: the value after the LAST
 '=' if one is present (so an arithmetic line like '105 - 105 = 0' commits 0,
 NOT the intermediate 105 — the loose 'oracle appears anywhere' false-positive
 the cold-read found), else the last rational. Returns (value|None, all)."""
 cands = _rationals(zone)
 if not cands:
 return None, []
 norm = _normalize(zone)
 if "=" in norm:
 tail = _rationals(norm.rsplit("=", 1)[1])
 if tail:
 return tail[-1], cands
 # no '=': the committed value is the FIRST rational (right after the marker /
 # at the head of the line) — a trailing parenthetical like "0 (note 105)" or
 # "253 (> 200)" must not steal the verdict.
 return cands[0], cands

def match_exact(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction. The COMMITTED final must equal it EXACTLY (no tolerance
 — rounded-only decimals are insufficient per the gradingscheme). Grades the
 committed value, not any number in the zone (no substring/intermediate pass)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 if committed == oracle:
 return True, "committed %s == oracle %s" % (committed, oracle)
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (cands %s)" % (
 committed, oracle, [str(c) for c in cands])

def match_vector(response, oracle):
 """oracle: list[Fraction]. Every component must appear in the final zone."""
 cands = set(_rationals(_final_zone(response)))
 missing = [str(o) for o in oracle if o not in cands]
 if not missing:
 return True, "all %d components present" % len(oracle)
 return False, "missing/incorrect components: %s" % missing

def match_rate(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction rate (e.g. 39/125). Accept the exact fraction, the
 decimal (tol 1e-4, the percent form is inherently rounded), or the percent
 (bare integer like 31.2 read as 31.2%)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 r = oracle
 rf = float(r)
 cf = float(committed)
 if committed == r:
 return True, "exact %s == oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf) < 1e-4:
 return True, "decimal %s ~= oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf * 100) < 1e-2:
 return True, "percent %s ~= oracle %s%%" % (committed, float(r * 100))
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (=%.6g; cands %s)" % (
 committed, r, rf, [str(c) for c in cands])


def grade(response: str, context: dict) -> dict:
 """Linearly Separable Dichotomies (Cover Counting)

 Oracle: [Fraction(464), Fraction(29,256)] (author answerkey, sympy-reconfirmed). Both the integer
 count and the reduced fraction must appear in the committed final answer
 (an unreduced equivalent like 464/4096 is accepted — Fraction reduces it)."""
 ok, reason = match_vector(response, [Fraction(464), Fraction(29,256)])
 return {"pass": bool(ok), "score": 1.0 if ok else 0.0, "notes": reason}


if __name__ == '__main__':
 import sys, json
 data = json.load(sys.stdin)
 out = grade(response=data['response'], context=data['context'])
 print(json.dumps(out))

#02

Asymptotic Fano Factor of a Refractory Poisson Spike Train

📄 Submission snapshot — parsed source evidence (private bank, unreleased; do not redistribute)
A neuron emits spikes according to a renewal process with an \emph{absolute refractory period} (dead time). After each spike the neuron is silent for a fixed $\Delta = 2~\mathrm{ms}$, and then the time to the next spike is exponential with rate $\lambda = 100~\mathrm{Hz}$. Thus successive interspike intervals (ISIs) are i.i.d.\ with the distribution of $\Delta + E$, where $E \sim \mathrm{Exponential}(\lambda)$. Let $N(t)$ be the number of spikes in $[0,t]$. \begin{enumerate} \item Give the stationary mean firing rate (in Hz).strong: Asking for the mean rate first gives a low-stakes warm-up that also lets graders partial-credit the harder part. \item Give the limiting Fano factor $\displaystyle F = \lim_{t\to\infty}\frac{\operatorname{Var}N(t)}{\mathbb{E}\,N(t)}$, as an exact reduced fraction and as a decimalpraise: Demanding both forms kills the ambiguity that usually plagues numerical-answer problems.. \end{enumerate}

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

For a renewal process the limiting Fano factor of the counting process equals the squared coefficient of variation of the ISI, $F = \mathrm{CV}^2 = \operatorname{Var}(\mathrm{ISI})/\mathbb{E}[\mathrm{ISI}]^2$ (this follows from the renewal central limit theorem / Smith's theorem: $\operatorname{Var}N(t)\sim \sigma^2 t/\mu^3$ and $\mathbb{E}N(t)\sim t/\mu$, so $F\to \sigma^2/\mu^2$). Here $\mathbb{E}[\mathrm{ISI}] = \Delta + 1/\lambda$ and $\operatorname{Var}(\mathrm{ISI}) = \operatorname{Var}(E) = 1/\lambda^2$ (a constant shift does not change the variance). \begin{itemize} \item Mean rate $= \dfrac{1}{\Delta + 1/\lambda} = \dfrac{1}{0.002 + 0.01} = \dfrac{1}{0.012} = 83.\overline{3}~\mathrm{Hz}$. \item Fano factor: \[ F = \mathrm{CV}^2 = \frac{1/\lambda^2}{(\Delta + 1/\lambda)^2} = \frac{1}{(1+\lambda\Delta)^2} = \frac{1}{(1+0.2)^2} = \frac{1}{1.44} = \boxed{\dfrac{25}{36}} \approx 0.6944. \] \end{itemize} The dead time makes the train \emph{sub-Poisson} ($F<1$); a memoryless Poisson process would give $F=1$.

Rubric (grading scheme) — how a verifier is meant to grade an answer.

\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: mean rate $=250/3 \approx 83.33~\mathrm{Hz}$; Fano factor $=25/36 \approx 0.6944$. \item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.001$ on the decimal Fano factor; rate within $\pm 0.1~\mathrm{Hz}$. \item Acceptable equivalent forms: $25/36$, $1/1.44$, $1/(1+\lambda\Delta)^2$ with the numbers substituted. \item Deterministic verifier: compare to $25/36$; a long-time Monte Carlo of the renewal counts reproduces $F\approx 0.69$ (see \path{_verify.py}). \end{itemize} Mark incorrect: reporting $F=\mathrm{CV}=5/6\approx0.833$ (used CV not CV$^2$); reporting $F=1$ (ignored the dead time); or using $\operatorname{Var}(\mathrm{ISI})=(\Delta+1/\lambda)^2$ (treated the whole ISI as exponential).

\begin{document}

\problemsetup
 {Asymptotic Fano Factor of a Refractory Poisson Spike Train}
 {Statistics}
 {Point processes / renewal theory}
 {Qualifying-exam-plus}
 {Contributor 02 (Dongyu Gong)}
 {\href{mailto:dongyu.gong@yale.edu}{dongyu.gong@yale.edu}}
 {Ready for review}
\problemtags
 {compute}
 {renewal-process variability, distinguishing Fano factor from CV}
 {exact match (reduced fraction) plus numerical tolerance}
 {reporting CV instead of CV$^2$, or assuming Poisson Fano $=1$ despite the dead time}
\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
A neuron emits spikes according to a renewal process with an \emph{absolute refractory period} (dead time). After each spike the neuron is silent for a fixed $\Delta = 2~\mathrm{ms}$, and then the time to the next spike is exponential with rate $\lambda = 100~\mathrm{Hz}$. Thus successive interspike intervals (ISIs) are i.i.d.\ with the distribution of $\Delta + E$, where $E \sim \mathrm{Exponential}(\lambda)$.

Let $N(t)$ be the number of spikes in $[0,t]$.
\begin{enumerate}
\item Give the stationary mean firing rate (in Hz).
\item Give the limiting Fano factor $\displaystyle F = \lim_{t\to\infty}\frac{\operatorname{Var}N(t)}{\mathbb{E}\,N(t)}$, as an exact reduced fraction and as a decimal.
\end{enumerate}
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
For a renewal process the limiting Fano factor of the counting process equals the squared coefficient of variation of the ISI, $F = \mathrm{CV}^2 = \operatorname{Var}(\mathrm{ISI})/\mathbb{E}[\mathrm{ISI}]^2$ (this follows from the renewal central limit theorem / Smith's theorem: $\operatorname{Var}N(t)\sim \sigma^2 t/\mu^3$ and $\mathbb{E}N(t)\sim t/\mu$, so $F\to \sigma^2/\mu^2$).

Here $\mathbb{E}[\mathrm{ISI}] = \Delta + 1/\lambda$ and $\operatorname{Var}(\mathrm{ISI}) = \operatorname{Var}(E) = 1/\lambda^2$ (a constant shift does not change the variance).
\begin{itemize}
\item Mean rate $= \dfrac{1}{\Delta + 1/\lambda} = \dfrac{1}{0.002 + 0.01} = \dfrac{1}{0.012} = 83.\overline{3}~\mathrm{Hz}$.
\item Fano factor:
\[
F = \mathrm{CV}^2 = \frac{1/\lambda^2}{(\Delta + 1/\lambda)^2} = \frac{1}{(1+\lambda\Delta)^2}
= \frac{1}{(1+0.2)^2} = \frac{1}{1.44} = \boxed{\dfrac{25}{36}} \approx 0.6944.
\]
\end{itemize}
The dead time makes the train \emph{sub-Poisson} ($F<1$); a memoryless Poisson process would give $F=1$.
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: mean rate $=250/3 \approx 83.33~\mathrm{Hz}$; Fano factor $=25/36 \approx 0.6944$.
\item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.001$ on the decimal Fano factor; rate within $\pm 0.1~\mathrm{Hz}$.
\item Acceptable equivalent forms: $25/36$, $1/1.44$, $1/(1+\lambda\Delta)^2$ with the numbers substituted.
\item Deterministic verifier: compare to $25/36$; a long-time Monte Carlo of the renewal counts reproduces $F\approx 0.69$ (see \path{_verify.py}).
\end{itemize}
Mark incorrect: reporting $F=\mathrm{CV}=5/6\approx0.833$ (used CV not CV$^2$); reporting $F=1$ (ignored the dead time); or using $\operatorname{Var}(\mathrm{ISI})=(\Delta+1/\lambda)^2$ (treated the whole ISI as exponential).
\end{gradingscheme}

\begin{editornotes}
Two compounding traps: (i) Fano factor is CV\textsuperscript{2}, not CV; (ii) the additive dead time changes the mean but not the variance, so the train is sub-Poisson. Models frequently anchor on ``Poisson $\Rightarrow$ Fano $=1$'' and miss that the refractory period breaks memorylessness.
\end{editornotes}

\end{msbproblem}

\end{document}

## Problem: Asymptotic Fano Factor of a Refractory Poisson Spike Train ### Setup Each interspike interval (ISI) is X = Δ + E, where Δ = 2 ms = 0.002 s (fixed dead time) and E ~ Exponential(λ) with λ = 100 Hz. Successive ISIs are i.i.d. ### Part 1: Stationary Mean Firing Rate By the renewal reward theorem, the long-run firing rate equals 1/E[X]. E[X] = Δ + 1/λ = 0.002 + 1/100 = 0.002 + 0.01 = 0.012 s Mean firing rate r = 1/0.012 = 1000/12 = 250/3 ≈ 83.33 Hz. ### Part 2: Limiting Fano Factor **Theoretical basis:** By Smith's theorem (renewal central limit theorem), for a renewal process with ISI mean μ and variance σ²: - E[N(t)] ~ t/μ as t → ∞ - Var[N(t)] ~ σ²t/μ³ as t → ∞ Therefore the limiting Fano factor is: F = lim_{t→∞} Var[N(t)] / E[N(t)] = (σ²t/μ³) / (t/μ) = σ²/μ² = CV² **Computing ISI moments:** Mean: μ = Δ + 1/λ = 0.002 + 0.01 = 0.012 s Variance: Since X = Δ + E and Δ is a constant, Var(X) = Var(E) = 1/λ² = 1/10000 = 10⁻⁴ s². (A constant shift does not affect the variance.) **Computing F:** F = σ²/μ² = (1/λ²) / (Δ + 1/λ)² = 1 / (λΔ + 1)² Substituting λΔ = 100 × 0.002 = 0.2: F = 1 / (0.2 + 1)² = 1 / (1.2)² = 1 / 1.44 = 100/144 = 25/36 ≈ 0.6944 **Interpretation:** The dead time Δ reduces the Fano factor below 1 (F = 1 for a pure Poisson process). The refractory period regularizes inter-spike timing, making the train sub-Poisson. The mean ISI is increased by Δ but the variance is unchanged (since Δ is a constant offset), so CV² < 1. ### Answers 1. Mean firing rate = 250/3 Hz ≈ 83.33 Hz 2. Limiting Fano factor F = 25/36 ≈ 0.6944

response
**Setup.** ISI = Δ + E, with Δ = 2 ms = 0.002 s (constant) and E ~ Exponential(λ = 100 Hz), so E[E] = 1/λ = 0.01 s and Var(E) = 1/λ² = 1e-4 s². Spikes form a renewal process. **(1) Stationary mean firing rate.** Mean ISI: μ = E[Δ + E] = Δ + 1/λ = 0.002 + 0.01 = 0.012 s. Rate = 1/μ = 1/0.012 = **250/3 Hz ≈ 83.33 Hz**. **(2) Limiting Fano factor.** For a renewal point process, the asymptotic (long-time) Fano factor equals the squared coefficient of variation of the ISI: F = lim_{t→∞} Var N(t)/E N(t) = CV² = Var(ISI)/μ². Since Δ is constant, Var(ISI) = Var(E) = 1/λ². Thus F = (1/λ²)/μ² = (1/(μλ))² = ((1/λ)/μ)² = (0.01/0.012)² = (5/6)² = **25/36 ≈ 0.6944**. A renewal Monte Carlo simulation gives mean rate ≈ 83.33 Hz and Fano ≈ 0.69, confirming both results. **Exact final answers:** (1) Mean firing rate = 250/3 Hz (≈ 83.33 Hz). (2) Limiting Fano factor F = 25/36.

Setup: ISI X = Δ + E with Δ = 2 ms = 1/500 s, E ~ Exp(λ=100 Hz). So E[X] = Δ + 1/λ = 1/500 + 1/100 = 3/250 s; Var(X) = Var(E) = 1/λ² = 1/10000 s² (Δ is a constant, contributes no variance). (1) Stationary mean firing rate = 1/E[X] = 1/(3/250) = 250/3 Hz ≈ 83.33 Hz. (2) For a renewal process, the limiting Fano factor F = lim Var N(t)/E N(t) = Var(X)/E[X]² = CV²(X) = (1/λ²)/(Δ+1/λ)² = (1/10000)/(9/62500) = 62500/90000 = 25/36. Equivalently [(1/λ)/(Δ+1/λ)]² = (5/6)² = 25/36. Final answers: (1) 250/3 Hz ≈ 83.33 Hz. (2) F = 25/36 (≈ 0.6944).

response
Problem: A neuron fires as a renewal process whose ISIs are i.i.d. with distribution Δ + E, where Δ = 2 ms = 0.002 s (absolute refractory/dead time) and E ~ Exponential(λ), λ = 100 Hz. Find (1) the stationary mean firing rate, and (2) the limiting Fano factor F = lim_{t→∞} Var N(t) / E N(t). Key facts about renewal processes: - The long-run rate equals 1/μ where μ = E[ISI] (elementary renewal theorem). - The limiting Fano factor of the counting process equals the squared coefficient of variation of the ISI: F = CV² = Var(ISI)/E[ISI]². This follows from the renewal CLT / Smith's theorem: Var N(t) ~ σ²t/μ³ and E N(t) ~ t/μ, so F → (σ²t/μ³)/(t/μ) = σ²/μ². ISI moments. Adding a constant Δ shifts the mean but leaves the variance unchanged: μ = E[ISI] = Δ + 1/λ σ² = Var(ISI) = Var(E) = 1/λ² (the dead time is deterministic, contributing 0 variance). Part 1 — Mean firing rate. μ = Δ + 1/λ = 0.002 + 1/100 = 0.002 + 0.01 = 0.012 s. rate = 1/μ = 1/0.012 = 250/3 ≈ 83.33 Hz (i.e., 83.3̄ Hz). Part 2 — Limiting Fano factor. F = CV² = σ²/μ² = (1/λ²)/(Δ + 1/λ)². Factor out 1/λ from the denominator: Δ + 1/λ = (1 + λΔ)/λ, so (Δ + 1/λ)² = (1+λΔ)²/λ², giving F = (1/λ²) · λ²/(1+λΔ)² = 1/(1+λΔ)². Numerically λΔ = 100 × 0.002 = 0.2, so F = 1/(1.2)² = 1/1.44 = 100/144 = 25/36 ≈ 0.6944. Interpretation / trap-checks: - F = CV², not CV. (CV = 5/6 ≈ 0.833; the Fano factor is its square, 25/36.) - The refractory period makes the process sub-Poisson, F < 1; a memoryless Poisson process would give F = 1. Here Δ regularizes the spacing, reducing count variability. - Var(ISI) is 1/λ² (not the whole-exponential value (Δ+1/λ)²); the additive dead time changes the mean only. Numerical sanity check (Monte Carlo of the renewal counts over long windows) reproduces mean rate ≈ 83.3 Hz and a sub-Poisson Fano factor consistent with ≈ 0.69 (finite-window/finite-trial noise nudges the estimate upward, but it is clearly below 1 and near the analytic value). Final answers: 1) Mean rate = 250/3 ≈ 83.33 Hz. 2) F = 1/(1+λΔ)² = 25/36 ≈ 0.6944.

  • Summary: Hypothesized pattern: (i) conflating Fano factor with CV and reporting `5/6 ~ 0.833`; (ii) anchoring on "Poisson => Fano = 1" and ignoring that the dead time makes the train a non-memoryless renewal process; (iii) incorrectly setting Var(ISI) = (Delta + 1/lambda)^2 by treating the whole ISI as exponential. The shift-invariance of variance (a constant Delta does not change the variance) is the subtle step most likely to be missed.

"""Deterministic verifier — contributor_02 / problem_02.
Asymptotic Fano Factor of a Refractory Poisson Train. Hand-authored (Claude Code, autonomous; via @yulinl2).
"""

import re
from fractions import Fraction

_MARKERS = [
 r"final\s+answer\s*[:=]?", r"the\s+(?:exact\s+)?answer\s+is\s*[:=]?",
 r"answer\s*[:=]", r"the\s+rate\s+is\s*[:=]?", r"therefore[,:]?",
 r"thus[,:]?", r"hence[,:]?",
]
_MARKER_RE = re.compile("|".join(_MARKERS), re.IGNORECASE)

def _boxed_inner(s):
 """Brace-balanced contents of every \\boxed{...} (handles nested \\frac)."""
 out, i = [], 0
 while True:
 j = s.find("boxed", i)
 if j < 0:
 break
 k = s.find("{", j)
 if k < 0:
 break
 depth, m = 0, k
 while m < len(s):
 if s[m] == "{":
 depth += 1
 elif s[m] == "}":
 depth -= 1
 if depth == 0:
 break
 m += 1
 out.append(s[k + 1:m])
 i = m + 1
 return out

def _normalize(s):
 """LaTeX -> plain so rationals survive: \\frac{a}{b} -> a/b, keep %.
 (c09 fractions are always integer/integer, so the flat a/b form is exact;
 a compound numerator would need parens, not present here.)"""
 # capture an optional leading minus OUTSIDE the frac (-\frac{a}{b} -> -a/b):
 # a detached "-" would otherwise be split off and the component read positive.
 s = re.sub(r"(-?)\s*\\[dt]?frac\s*\{([^{}]*)\}\s*\{([^{}]*)\}", r" \1\2/\3 ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\(left|right|!|,|;|:|quad|qquad)", " ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\text\s*\{[^{}]*\}", " ", s)
 s = s.replace(r"\%", "%").replace(r"\$", " ").replace("$", " ")
 s = s.replace("{,}", "") # 225{,}000 -> 225000
 s = s.replace("\\", " ").replace("{", " ").replace("}", " ")
 # thousands separator ONLY when strictly adjacent (1,250 -> 1250); never join
 # across a space (a vector's "1907, 379" must stay two numbers).
 s = re.sub(r"(\d),(\d{3})(?!\d)", r"\1\2", s)
 return s

# token: percent | fraction a/b | decimal | integer (left-to-right, non-overlap)
_TOK = re.compile(
 r"(?P<pct>-?\d+(?:\.\d+)?)\s*%"
 r"|(?P<n>-?\d+)\s*/\s*(?P<d>-?\d+)"
 r"|(?P<dec>-?\d+\.\d+)"
 r"|(?P<int>-?\d+)")

def _rationals(zone):
 """Ordered list of Fraction values committed in `zone` (already a string)."""
 out = []
 for m in _TOK.finditer(_normalize(zone)):
 try:
 if m.group("pct") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("pct")) / 100)
 elif m.group("n") is not None:
 d = int(m.group("d"))
 if d == 0:
 continue
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("n")), d))
 elif m.group("dec") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("dec")))
 else:
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("int"))))
 except (ValueError, ZeroDivisionError):
 continue
 return out

def _final_zone(response):
 """The text of the model's committed final answer (most -> least reliable):
 boxed contents; else the last marker line's tail; else the last line."""
 if not response or not response.strip:
 return ""
 boxed = _boxed_inner(response)
 if boxed:
 return " ".join(boxed)
 lines = [ln for ln in response.splitlines if ln.strip]
 # last marker line that actually carries a number (weak discourse markers
 # like "therefore/thus/hence" otherwise hijack the zone to a number-less
 # trailing comment, burying the real commit on the line above).
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 m = _MARKER_RE.search(ln)
 if m and any(c.isdigit for c in ln[m.start:]):
 return ln[m.start:]
 # else the last line that carries a number
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 if any(c.isdigit for c in ln):
 return ln
 return lines[-1] if lines else ""

def _committed_rational(zone):
 """The single COMMITTED final rational in `zone`: the value after the LAST
 '=' if one is present (so an arithmetic line like '105 - 105 = 0' commits 0,
 NOT the intermediate 105 — the loose 'oracle appears anywhere' false-positive
 the cold-read found), else the last rational. Returns (value|None, all)."""
 cands = _rationals(zone)
 if not cands:
 return None, []
 norm = _normalize(zone)
 if "=" in norm:
 tail = _rationals(norm.rsplit("=", 1)[1])
 if tail:
 return tail[-1], cands
 # no '=': the committed value is the FIRST rational (right after the marker /
 # at the head of the line) — a trailing parenthetical like "0 (note 105)" or
 # "253 (> 200)" must not steal the verdict.
 return cands[0], cands

def match_exact(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction. The COMMITTED final must equal it EXACTLY (no tolerance
 — rounded-only decimals are insufficient per the gradingscheme). Grades the
 committed value, not any number in the zone (no substring/intermediate pass)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 if committed == oracle:
 return True, "committed %s == oracle %s" % (committed, oracle)
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (cands %s)" % (
 committed, oracle, [str(c) for c in cands])

def match_vector(response, oracle):
 """oracle: list[Fraction]. Every component must appear in the final zone."""
 cands = set(_rationals(_final_zone(response)))
 missing = [str(o) for o in oracle if o not in cands]
 if not missing:
 return True, "all %d components present" % len(oracle)
 return False, "missing/incorrect components: %s" % missing

def match_rate(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction rate (e.g. 39/125). Accept the exact fraction, the
 decimal (tol 1e-4, the percent form is inherently rounded), or the percent
 (bare integer like 31.2 read as 31.2%)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 r = oracle
 rf = float(r)
 cf = float(committed)
 if committed == r:
 return True, "exact %s == oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf) < 1e-4:
 return True, "decimal %s ~= oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf * 100) < 1e-2:
 return True, "percent %s ~= oracle %s%%" % (committed, float(r * 100))
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (=%.6g; cands %s)" % (
 committed, r, rf, [str(c) for c in cands])


def grade(response: str, context: dict) -> dict:
 """Asymptotic Fano Factor of a Refractory Poisson Train

 Oracle: Fraction(25,36) (author answerkey, sympy-reconfirmed). Accept the EXACT
 reduced fraction OR a 4-decimal value (tol 5e-5); a coarsely-rounded
 decimal alone is insufficient (gradingscheme asks for both forms)."""
 oracle = Fraction(25,36)
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return {"pass": False, "score": 0.0, "notes": "no rational final answer extracted"}
 if committed == oracle or abs(float(committed) - float(oracle)) < 5e-5:
 return {"pass": True, "score": 1.0,
 "notes": "committed %s matches oracle %s" % (committed, oracle)}
 return {"pass": False, "score": 0.0,
 "notes": "committed %s != oracle %s (cands %s)" % (committed, oracle, [str(c) for c in cands])}


if __name__ == '__main__':
 import sys, json
 data = json.load(sys.stdin)
 out = grade(response=data['response'], context=data['context'])
 print(json.dumps(out))

#03

Choice Probability in a Drifting Diffusion Decision Model

📄 Submission snapshot — parsed source evidence (private bank, unreleased; do not redistribute)
A two-alternative decisionambiguity: The DDM framing is decorative — the problem reduces to a textbook exit-probability question, raising contamination risk without adding cognitive load. is modeled by accumulated evidence $X_t$ following the It\^o diffusion \[ dX_t = v\,dt + \sigma\,dW_t,\qquad X_0 = z, \] with constant drift $v>0$ and diffusion coefficient $\sigma^2>0$. The decision terminates when $X_t$ first reaches the upper absorbing boundary $a>0$ (``choose A'') or the lower absorbing boundary $0$ (``choose B''). Assume the process starts at the midpoint $z = a/2$.suggestion: Fixing z=a/2 in the setup is what makes this a known one-liner for frontier models. Asking for general z (and only then specialising) would raise difficulty and reduce recognisability. Let $p$ be the probability that the upper boundary is reached first. \begin{enumerate} \item Derive $p$ in closed form for general $v,\sigma^2,a$ at the symmetric start $z=a/2$. \item Evaluate $p$ for $v=1$, $\sigma^2=1$, $a=2$ (give the exact closed form and a 4-decimal value). \item State $\lim_{v\to 0^+} p$ and explain in one line why it must equal that value. \end{enumerate}

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

The process $M_t = \exp(-2v X_t/\sigma^2)$ is a martingale (its drift cancels by It\^o's formula). With absorption at $0$ and $a$, optional stopping gives $\mathbb{E}[M_\tau] = M_0$, i.e. \[ p\,e^{-2va/\sigma^2} + (1-p)\cdot 1 = e^{-2vz/\sigma^2} \;\Longrightarrow\; p = \frac{e^{-2vz/\sigma^2}-1}{e^{-2va/\sigma^2}-1}. \] At the symmetric start $z=a/2$, write $c = va/\sigma^2$. Then $e^{-2vz/\sigma^2}=e^{-c}$ and $e^{-2va/\sigma^2}=e^{-2c}=(e^{-c})^2$, so \[ p = \frac{e^{-c}-1}{e^{-2c}-1} = \frac{e^{-c}-1}{(e^{-c}-1)(e^{-c}+1)} = \frac{1}{1+e^{-c}} = \frac{1}{1+e^{-va/\sigma^2}}. \] This is exactly a logistic function of the drift-to-noise quantity $va/\sigma^2$. For $v=1,\sigma^2=1,a=2$: $c=2$, so \[ p = \frac{1}{1+e^{-2}} \approx 0.8808. \] As $v\to 0^+$, $c\to 0$ and $p\to \tfrac{1}{1+e^{0}}=\tfrac12$; with no drift and a symmetric start the two boundaries are equally likely by symmetry.

Rubric (grading scheme) — how a verifier is meant to grade an answer.

\textbf{Mode A: closed form plus numeric.} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: $p = \dfrac{1}{1+e^{-va/\sigma^2}}$; for the given numbers $p = \dfrac{1}{1+e^{-2}}\approx 0.8808$; limit $=1/2$. \item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.0005$ on $0.8808$. \item Acceptable equivalent forms: $\dfrac{e^{-2vz/\sigma^2}-1}{e^{-2va/\sigma^2}-1}$ evaluated at $z=a/2$, or $\dfrac{e^{c}}{1+e^{c}}$ with $c=va/\sigma^2$. \item Deterministic verifier: closed-form match plus Monte-Carlo first-passage simulation giving $p\approx0.88$ (see \path{_verify.py}). \end{itemize} Mark incorrect: any sign flip giving $p = 1/(1+e^{+2})\approx 0.119$; using $p=z/a=1/2$ (driftless linear formula); or omitting the factor $2$ in the exponent.

\documentclass[../../../main.tex]{subfiles}
\begin{document}

% Keep this file in sync with the matching problem_03_review.md sidecar.

\problemsetup
 {Choice Probability in a Drifting Diffusion Decision Model}
 {Statistics / Mathematics}
 {Stochastic processes / first-passage problems}
 {Qualifying-exam-plus}
 {Contributor 02 (Dongyu Gong)}
 {\href{mailto:dongyu.gong@yale.edu}{dongyu.gong@yale.edu}}
 {Ready for review}
\problemtags
 {compute / derive}
 {boundary-value reasoning for diffusions, optional stopping with the right martingale}
 {exact match (closed form) plus numerical tolerance}
 {sign error in the drift exponent, or using the driftless symmetric formula}
\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
A two-alternative decision is modeled by accumulated evidence $X_t$ following the It\^o diffusion
\[
dX_t = v\,dt + \sigma\,dW_t,\qquad X_0 = z,
\]
with constant drift $v>0$ and diffusion coefficient $\sigma^2>0$. The decision terminates when $X_t$ first reaches the upper absorbing boundary $a>0$ (``choose A'') or the lower absorbing boundary $0$ (``choose B''). Assume the process starts at the midpoint $z = a/2$.

Let $p$ be the probability that the upper boundary is reached first.
\begin{enumerate}
\item Derive $p$ in closed form for general $v,\sigma^2,a$ at the symmetric start $z=a/2$.
\item Evaluate $p$ for $v=1$, $\sigma^2=1$, $a=2$ (give the exact closed form and a 4-decimal value).
\item State $\lim_{v\to 0^+} p$ and explain in one line why it must equal that value.
\end{enumerate}
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
The process $M_t = \exp(-2v X_t/\sigma^2)$ is a martingale (its drift cancels by It\^o's formula). With absorption at $0$ and $a$, optional stopping gives $\mathbb{E}[M_\tau] = M_0$, i.e.
\[
p\,e^{-2va/\sigma^2} + (1-p)\cdot 1 = e^{-2vz/\sigma^2}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
p = \frac{e^{-2vz/\sigma^2}-1}{e^{-2va/\sigma^2}-1}.
\]
At the symmetric start $z=a/2$, write $c = va/\sigma^2$. Then $e^{-2vz/\sigma^2}=e^{-c}$ and $e^{-2va/\sigma^2}=e^{-2c}=(e^{-c})^2$, so
\[
p = \frac{e^{-c}-1}{e^{-2c}-1} = \frac{e^{-c}-1}{(e^{-c}-1)(e^{-c}+1)} = \frac{1}{1+e^{-c}}
= \frac{1}{1+e^{-va/\sigma^2}}.
\]
This is exactly a logistic function of the drift-to-noise quantity $va/\sigma^2$.

For $v=1,\sigma^2=1,a=2$: $c=2$, so
\[
p = \frac{1}{1+e^{-2}} \approx 0.8808.
\]
As $v\to 0^+$, $c\to 0$ and $p\to \tfrac{1}{1+e^{0}}=\tfrac12$; with no drift and a symmetric start the two boundaries are equally likely by symmetry.
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: closed form plus numeric.}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: $p = \dfrac{1}{1+e^{-va/\sigma^2}}$; for the given numbers $p = \dfrac{1}{1+e^{-2}}\approx 0.8808$; limit $=1/2$.
\item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.0005$ on $0.8808$.
\item Acceptable equivalent forms: $\dfrac{e^{-2vz/\sigma^2}-1}{e^{-2va/\sigma^2}-1}$ evaluated at $z=a/2$, or $\dfrac{e^{c}}{1+e^{c}}$ with $c=va/\sigma^2$.
\item Deterministic verifier: closed-form match plus Monte-Carlo first-passage simulation giving $p\approx0.88$ (see \path{_verify.py}).
\end{itemize}
Mark incorrect: any sign flip giving $p = 1/(1+e^{+2})\approx 0.119$; using $p=z/a=1/2$ (driftless linear formula); or omitting the factor $2$ in the exponent.
\end{gradingscheme}

\begin{editornotes}
The clean logistic collapse only happens at the symmetric start, and the most common error is a sign error in the exponent of the exponential martingale, which silently swaps the two boundaries. The $v\to0$ check is a built-in trap detector.
\end{editornotes}

\end{msbproblem}

\end{document}

## Problem: Choice Probability in a Drifting Diffusion Decision Model **Setup:** $dX_t = v\,dt + \sigma\,dW_t$, $X_0 = z = a/2$, absorbing boundaries at $0$ (lower, "choose B") and $a$ (upper, "choose A"). Find $p = P(\text{upper boundary reached first})$. --- ### Part 1: Closed-form derivation for general $v, \sigma^2, a$ at $z = a/2$ **Key step — find an exponential martingale.** Consider $M_t = e^{-2vX_t/\sigma^2}$. Applying Ito's formula with $f(x) = e^{-2vx/\sigma^2}$, so $f'(x) = -\frac{2v}{\sigma^2}f(x)$ and $f''(x) = \frac{4v^2}{\sigma^4}f(x)$: $$dM_t = f'(X_t)\,dX_t + \tfrac{1}{2}f''(X_t)(dX_t)^2$$ Substituting $dX_t = v\,dt + \sigma\,dW_t$ and $(dX_t)^2 = \sigma^2\,dt$: $$dM_t = M_t\!\left(-\frac{2v}{\sigma^2}\right)(v\,dt + \sigma\,dW_t) + \frac{1}{2}M_t\cdot\frac{4v^2}{\sigma^4}\cdot\sigma^2\,dt$$ $$= M_t\!\left(-\frac{2v^2}{\sigma^2} + \frac{2v^2}{\sigma^2}\right)dt + M_t\!\left(-\frac{2v}{\sigma}\right)dW_t = M_t\!\left(-\frac{2v}{\sigma}\right)dW_t$$ The $dt$ drift cancels exactly, so $M_t$ is a local martingale. On $[0,a]$ the process is bounded, so $M_t$ is a true martingale. **Optional Stopping.** Let $\tau = \inf\{t \geq 0: X_t \in \{0, a\}\}$. By OST: $$\mathbb{E}[M_\tau] = M_0 = e^{-2vz/\sigma^2}$$ At stopping time, $M_\tau = e^{-2va/\sigma^2}$ with probability $p$, or $M_\tau = e^0 = 1$ with probability $1-p$. Thus: $$p\cdot e^{-2va/\sigma^2} + (1-p)\cdot 1 = e^{-2vz/\sigma^2}$$ Solving for $p$: $$p = \frac{e^{-2vz/\sigma^2} - 1}{e^{-2va/\sigma^2} - 1}$$ **Symmetric start $z = a/2$:** Let $c = va/\sigma^2$. Then $e^{-2vz/\sigma^2} = e^{-c}$ and $e^{-2va/\sigma^2} = e^{-2c}$: $$p = \frac{e^{-c} - 1}{e^{-2c} - 1} = \frac{e^{-c} - 1}{(e^{-c}-1)(e^{-c}+1)} = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-c}}$$ $$\boxed{p = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-va/\sigma^2}}}$$ This is the logistic function of the drift-to-noise ratio $c = va/\sigma^2$. --- ### Part 2: Numerical evaluation at $v=1$, $\sigma^2=1$, $a=2$ Here $c = va/\sigma^2 = (1)(2)/(1) = 2$. $$p = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-2}}$$ Numerically: $e^{-2} \approx 0.13534$, so $p \approx 1/1.13534 \approx \mathbf{0.8808}$. --- ### Part 3: Limit as $v \to 0^+$ As $v \to 0^+$, $c = va/\sigma^2 \to 0$, so $e^{-c} \to 1$: $$\lim_{v \to 0^+} p = \frac{1}{1 + e^0} = \frac{1}{2}$$ **One-line explanation:** With zero drift the process is a symmetric martingale; starting at the midpoint $z = a/2$, both boundaries are equidistant and equally likely to be hit first by symmetry.

Setup: p(z) = P(hit a before 0 | X0 = z) solves the backward equation (σ²/2)p'' + v p' = 0 with p(0)=0, p(a)=1. Solving: with λ = 2v/σ², general solution p(z) = A + B e^{-λz}. Applying boundary conditions gives the general closed form: p(z) = (1 - e^{-2vz/σ²}) / (1 - e^{-2va/σ²}). (1) At z = a/2: let θ = 2va/σ². Then p(a/2) = (1 - e^{-θ/2})/(1 - e^{-θ}) = 1/(1 + e^{-θ/2}), so p(a/2) = 1 / (1 + e^{-va/σ²}) = ½(1 + tanh(va/(2σ²))). This is a logistic (sigmoid) in the dimensionless drift va/σ². (2) For v=1, σ²=1, a=2: va/σ² = 2, so p = 1/(1 + e^{-2}) = e²/(e² + 1) ≈ 0.8807970780. (Verified numerically: 0.88079707797788.)

response
**Setup.** For dX = v dt + σ dW with absorbing barriers at 0 and a, the hitting probability p(z) = P(reach a before 0 | X₀ = z) satisfies the backward (generator) ODE v·p'(z) + (σ²/2)·p''(z) = 0, p(0) = 0, p(a) = 1. **General solution.** Try p' = C·e^{-(2v/σ²)z}. Let θ = 2v/σ². Integrating and applying p(0)=0, p(a)=1 gives p(z) = (1 − e^{−θz}) / (1 − e^{−θa}), θ = 2v/σ². **(1) At z = a/2.** Using 1 − e^{−θa} = (1 − e^{−θa/2})(1 + e^{−θa/2}): p = (1 − e^{−θa/2}) / [(1 − e^{−θa/2})(1 + e^{−θa/2})] = 1/(1 + e^{−θa/2}). Since θa/2 = (2v/σ²)(a/2) = va/σ², **p = 1 / (1 + e^{−va/σ²})** (logistic in the dimensionless drift va/σ²). (Sanity check: as v→0 this →1/2, the symmetric random-walk value; v>0 gives p>1/2.) **(2) For v = 1, σ² = 1, a = 2.** Here va/σ² = 2, so **p = 1/(1 + e^{−2}) = e²/(e² + 1) ≈ 0.8807970779778824.**

Setup: p(z)=P(hit a before 0) solves (σ²/2)p'' + v p' = 0 with p(0)=0, p(a)=1. Let θ=2v/σ². General solution p(z)=A+B e^{-θz}, giving p(z) = (1 - e^{-θz}) / (1 - e^{-θa}), θ = 2v/σ². (1) At z = a/2: using 1 - e^{-θa} = (1 - e^{-θa/2})(1 + e^{-θa/2}), p = 1 / (1 + e^{-θa/2}) = 1 / (1 + e^{-va/σ²}). (This is a logistic/sigmoid form; v→0 gives 1/2, large v→+1, as expected.) (2) For v=1, σ²=1, a=2: va/σ² = 2, so p = 1/(1 + e^{-2}) = e²/(e²+1) ≈ 0.8807970779778824.

Problem: X_t follows dX_t = v dt + σ dW_t, v>0, σ²>0, absorbing boundaries at 0 (choose B) and a>0 (choose A), start z = a/2. p = P(hit a before 0). Part 1 — closed form. Use the exponential martingale M_t = exp(−2v X_t/σ²). By Itô, dM_t = M_t[ −(2v/σ²) dX_t + ½(2v/σ²)² (dX_t)² ] = M_t[ −(2v/σ²)(v dt + σ dW_t) + ½(4v²/σ⁴)σ² dt ] = M_t[ (−2v²/σ² + 2v²/σ²) dt − (2v/σ²)σ dW_t ] = −(2v/σ) M_t dW_t, so the drift cancels and M_t is a (bounded, since X_t∈[0,a] up to τ) martingale. The hitting time τ is a.s. finite (positive drift toward a, bounded domain), so optional stopping applies: E[M_τ] = M_0 = e^{−2vz/σ²}. M_τ takes value e^{−2va/σ²} with prob p (upper hit) and value e^{0}=1 with prob 1−p (lower hit): p·e^{−2va/σ²} + (1−p)·1 = e^{−2vz/σ²} ⇒ p = (e^{−2vz/σ²} − 1)/(e^{−2va/σ²} − 1). Symmetric start z = a/2. Let c = va/σ². Then e^{−2vz/σ²} = e^{−va/σ²} = e^{−c} and e^{−2va/σ²} = e^{−2c} = (e^{−c})². Factor: p = (e^{−c} − 1)/(e^{−2c} − 1) = (e^{−c} − 1)/[(e^{−c} − 1)(e^{−c} + 1)] = 1/(1 + e^{−c}) = 1/(1 + e^{−va/σ²}). This is a logistic function of the drift-to-noise quantity va/σ². Part 2 — numbers v=1, σ²=1, a=2. c = va/σ² = 2, so p = 1/(1 + e^{−2}) = e²/(1 + e²) ≈ 0.88079708 ≈ 0.8808. Closed-form check in Python gave 0.8807970779778823; a Monte-Carlo Euler–Maruyama first-passage simulation is consistent with ≈0.88. Part 3 — limit. As v → 0⁺, c = va/σ² → 0, so p → 1/(1 + e^{0}) = 1/2. Reason (one line): with zero drift and a symmetric start z = a/2, the problem is symmetric under reflection about a/2, so the upper and lower boundaries are reached first with equal probability. Common-error guards: sign in the exponent must give the logistic with +va/σ² (p≈0.8808), not 1/(1+e^{+2})≈0.119; do not use the driftless linear answer p=z/a=1/2; keep the factor 2 in the exponent.
🗒️ Contributor’s own notes / pasted responses

Your own review sidecar — the responses you collected and your own failure-mode read. Shown verbatim, as your words (distinct from the bot review and the model sweep below).

Your failure-mode summary:

  • Observed failure mode: sign error in the drift exponent, or using the driftless symmetric formula
  • Summary: Hypothesized pattern: the dominant error is a sign error in the exponent of the exponential martingale, which swaps the two boundaries and yields `1/(1+e^{+2}) ~ 0.119` instead of `0.8808` — a confidently wrong answer that even passes a superficial "between 0 and 1" sanity check. A secondary error is using the driftless linear hitting probability `z/a = 1/2`, ignoring the drift. The built-in `v->0 => 1/2` check is designed to expose the sign error.

"""Deterministic verifier — contributor_02 / problem_03.
Choice Probability in a Drifting Diffusion (Logistic of Drift). Hand-authored (Claude Code, autonomous; via @yulinl2).
"""

import re
from fractions import Fraction

_MARKERS = [
 r"final\s+answer\s*[:=]?", r"the\s+(?:exact\s+)?answer\s+is\s*[:=]?",
 r"answer\s*[:=]", r"the\s+rate\s+is\s*[:=]?", r"therefore[,:]?",
 r"thus[,:]?", r"hence[,:]?",
]
_MARKER_RE = re.compile("|".join(_MARKERS), re.IGNORECASE)

def _boxed_inner(s):
 """Brace-balanced contents of every \\boxed{...} (handles nested \\frac)."""
 out, i = [], 0
 while True:
 j = s.find("boxed", i)
 if j < 0:
 break
 k = s.find("{", j)
 if k < 0:
 break
 depth, m = 0, k
 while m < len(s):
 if s[m] == "{":
 depth += 1
 elif s[m] == "}":
 depth -= 1
 if depth == 0:
 break
 m += 1
 out.append(s[k + 1:m])
 i = m + 1
 return out

def _normalize(s):
 """LaTeX -> plain so rationals survive: \\frac{a}{b} -> a/b, keep %.
 (c09 fractions are always integer/integer, so the flat a/b form is exact;
 a compound numerator would need parens, not present here.)"""
 # capture an optional leading minus OUTSIDE the frac (-\frac{a}{b} -> -a/b):
 # a detached "-" would otherwise be split off and the component read positive.
 s = re.sub(r"(-?)\s*\\[dt]?frac\s*\{([^{}]*)\}\s*\{([^{}]*)\}", r" \1\2/\3 ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\(left|right|!|,|;|:|quad|qquad)", " ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\text\s*\{[^{}]*\}", " ", s)
 s = s.replace(r"\%", "%").replace(r"\$", " ").replace("$", " ")
 s = s.replace("{,}", "") # 225{,}000 -> 225000
 s = s.replace("\\", " ").replace("{", " ").replace("}", " ")
 # thousands separator ONLY when strictly adjacent (1,250 -> 1250); never join
 # across a space (a vector's "1907, 379" must stay two numbers).
 s = re.sub(r"(\d),(\d{3})(?!\d)", r"\1\2", s)
 return s

# token: percent | fraction a/b | decimal | integer (left-to-right, non-overlap)
_TOK = re.compile(
 r"(?P<pct>-?\d+(?:\.\d+)?)\s*%"
 r"|(?P<n>-?\d+)\s*/\s*(?P<d>-?\d+)"
 r"|(?P<dec>-?\d+\.\d+)"
 r"|(?P<int>-?\d+)")

def _rationals(zone):
 """Ordered list of Fraction values committed in `zone` (already a string)."""
 out = []
 for m in _TOK.finditer(_normalize(zone)):
 try:
 if m.group("pct") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("pct")) / 100)
 elif m.group("n") is not None:
 d = int(m.group("d"))
 if d == 0:
 continue
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("n")), d))
 elif m.group("dec") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("dec")))
 else:
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("int"))))
 except (ValueError, ZeroDivisionError):
 continue
 return out

def _final_zone(response):
 """The text of the model's committed final answer (most -> least reliable):
 boxed contents; else the last marker line's tail; else the last line."""
 if not response or not response.strip:
 return ""
 boxed = _boxed_inner(response)
 if boxed:
 return " ".join(boxed)
 lines = [ln for ln in response.splitlines if ln.strip]
 # last marker line that actually carries a number (weak discourse markers
 # like "therefore/thus/hence" otherwise hijack the zone to a number-less
 # trailing comment, burying the real commit on the line above).
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 m = _MARKER_RE.search(ln)
 if m and any(c.isdigit for c in ln[m.start:]):
 return ln[m.start:]
 # else the last line that carries a number
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 if any(c.isdigit for c in ln):
 return ln
 return lines[-1] if lines else ""

def _committed_rational(zone):
 """The single COMMITTED final rational in `zone`: the value after the LAST
 '=' if one is present (so an arithmetic line like '105 - 105 = 0' commits 0,
 NOT the intermediate 105 — the loose 'oracle appears anywhere' false-positive
 the cold-read found), else the last rational. Returns (value|None, all)."""
 cands = _rationals(zone)
 if not cands:
 return None, []
 norm = _normalize(zone)
 if "=" in norm:
 tail = _rationals(norm.rsplit("=", 1)[1])
 if tail:
 return tail[-1], cands
 # no '=': the committed value is the FIRST rational (right after the marker /
 # at the head of the line) — a trailing parenthetical like "0 (note 105)" or
 # "253 (> 200)" must not steal the verdict.
 return cands[0], cands

def match_exact(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction. The COMMITTED final must equal it EXACTLY (no tolerance
 — rounded-only decimals are insufficient per the gradingscheme). Grades the
 committed value, not any number in the zone (no substring/intermediate pass)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 if committed == oracle:
 return True, "committed %s == oracle %s" % (committed, oracle)
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (cands %s)" % (
 committed, oracle, [str(c) for c in cands])

def match_vector(response, oracle):
 """oracle: list[Fraction]. Every component must appear in the final zone."""
 cands = set(_rationals(_final_zone(response)))
 missing = [str(o) for o in oracle if o not in cands]
 if not missing:
 return True, "all %d components present" % len(oracle)
 return False, "missing/incorrect components: %s" % missing

def match_rate(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction rate (e.g. 39/125). Accept the exact fraction, the
 decimal (tol 1e-4, the percent form is inherently rounded), or the percent
 (bare integer like 31.2 read as 31.2%)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 r = oracle
 rf = float(r)
 cf = float(committed)
 if committed == r:
 return True, "exact %s == oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf) < 1e-4:
 return True, "decimal %s ~= oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf * 100) < 1e-2:
 return True, "percent %s ~= oracle %s%%" % (committed, float(r * 100))
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (=%.6g; cands %s)" % (
 committed, r, rf, [str(c) for c in cands])


def grade(response: str, context: dict) -> dict:
 """Choice Probability in a Drifting Diffusion (Logistic of Drift)

 Oracle: a TRANSCENDENTAL value (1/(1+e^{-2})); the problem asks for a
 4-decimal value, so the committed final is matched to 0.880797
 within tol 1e-3 (a reasonable rounding passes; the wrong-value distractors
 are all far away). A symbolic-only answer with no decimal is incomplete."""
 target = 0.8807970779778824
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return {"pass": False, "score": 0.0, "notes": "no numeric final answer extracted"}
 if abs(float(committed) - target) < 1e-3:
 return {"pass": True, "score": 1.0, "notes": "committed %s ~= %.4f" % (committed, target)}
 return {"pass": False, "score": 0.0,
 "notes": "committed %s != %.4f (cands %s)" % (committed, target, [str(c) for c in cands])}

 print(json.dumps(out))

#04

Steady-State Gain of a Scalar Kalman Filter

📄 Submission snapshot — parsed source evidence (private bank, unreleased; do not redistribute)
A scalar latent state evolves and is observed as \[ x_t = a\,x_{t-1} + w_t, \quad w_t \sim \mathcal{N}(0,q),\qquad y_t = x_t + v_t, \quad v_t \sim \mathcal{N}(0,r), \] with $w_t, v_t$ independent white noise. Take $a = 1$, $q = 1$, $r = 2$.suggestion: Consider a≠1 (e.g. a=0.9) so the a^2 term cannot be silently dropped — would harden against the failure mode you flag in editor notes and reduce contamination overlap with textbook instances. Run the Kalman filter to its steady state. Let $P^- = \lim_t \operatorname{Var}(x_t \mid y_{1:t-1})$ be the steady-state \emph{prior} (predicted) error variance, $P^+ = \lim_t \operatorname{Var}(x_t \mid y_{1:t})$ the steady-state \emph{posterior} (filtered) error variance, and $K = \lim_t K_t$ the steady-state Kalman gain. \begin{enumerate} \item Write the scalar algebraic Riccati equation for $P^-$ and solve it. \item Give $P^-$, $P^+$, and $K$ as exact numbers. \end{enumerate}

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

The recursion is: predict $P^-_t = a^2 P^+_{t-1} + q$; update $P^+_t = P^-_t - \dfrac{(P^-_t)^2}{P^-_t + r} = \dfrac{P^-_t\, r}{P^-_t + r}$, with gain $K_t = \dfrac{P^-_t}{P^-_t+r}$. In steady state, substitute $P^+ = P^- r/(P^-+r)$ into the prediction step. Writing $s = P^-$ and using $a=1$: \[ s = a^2\,\frac{s\,r}{s+r} + q = \frac{s r}{s+r} + q. \] Multiply by $(s+r)$: \[ s(s+r) = s r + q(s+r) \;\Longrightarrow\; s^2 + s r = s r + q s + q r \;\Longrightarrow\; s^2 - q s - q r = 0. \] With $q=1,r=2$: $s^2 - s - 2 = 0 \Rightarrow (s-2)(s+1)=0$. The positive (admissible) root is $s = 2$. Therefore \[ P^- = 2,\qquad P^+ = \frac{P^- r}{P^- + r} = \frac{2\cdot 2}{4} = 1, \qquad K = \frac{P^-}{P^- + r} = \frac{2}{4} = \boxed{\tfrac12}. \]

Rubric (grading scheme) — how a verifier is meant to grade an answer.

\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: $P^- = 2$, $P^+ = 1$, $K = 1/2$. \item Numerical tolerance: none (all rational); decimal $K=0.5$. \item Acceptable equivalent forms: $K=0.5$; reporting only $K=1/2$ with correct $P^-,P^+$ is full credit. \item Deterministic verifier: iterate the Riccati recursion to convergence and compare (see \path{_verify.py}); both roots of $s^2-s-2$ are checked and the negative root $s=-1$ rejected. \end{itemize} Mark incorrect: $K = P^+/(P^++r) = 1/3$ (used posterior in the gain); taking the root $s=-1$; or omitting $a^2$ (here $a=1$ hides it, but the algebra must show it).

\documentclass[../../../main.tex]{subfiles}
\begin{document}

% Keep this file in sync with the matching problem_04_review.md sidecar.

\problemsetup
 {Steady-State Gain of a Scalar Kalman Filter}
 {Statistics}
 {State-space models / discrete algebraic Riccati equation}
 {Qualifying-exam-plus}
 {Contributor 02 (Dongyu Gong)}
 {\href{mailto:dongyu.gong@yale.edu}{dongyu.gong@yale.edu}}
 {Ready for review}
\problemtags
 {compute / derive}
 {recursive-filter algebra, distinguishing prior from posterior error variance}
 {exact match (rational)}
 {confusing prior vs.\ posterior variance, dropping the dynamics coefficient, or solving the wrong Riccati root}
\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
A scalar latent state evolves and is observed as
\[
x_t = a\,x_{t-1} + w_t, \quad w_t \sim \mathcal{N}(0,q),\qquad
y_t = x_t + v_t, \quad v_t \sim \mathcal{N}(0,r),
\]
with $w_t, v_t$ independent white noise. Take $a = 1$, $q = 1$, $r = 2$.

Run the Kalman filter to its steady state. Let $P^- = \lim_t \operatorname{Var}(x_t \mid y_{1:t-1})$ be the steady-state \emph{prior} (predicted) error variance, $P^+ = \lim_t \operatorname{Var}(x_t \mid y_{1:t})$ the steady-state \emph{posterior} (filtered) error variance, and $K = \lim_t K_t$ the steady-state Kalman gain.
\begin{enumerate}
\item Write the scalar algebraic Riccati equation for $P^-$ and solve it.
\item Give $P^-$, $P^+$, and $K$ as exact numbers.
\end{enumerate}
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
The recursion is: predict $P^-_t = a^2 P^+_{t-1} + q$; update $P^+_t = P^-_t - \dfrac{(P^-_t)^2}{P^-_t + r} = \dfrac{P^-_t\, r}{P^-_t + r}$, with gain $K_t = \dfrac{P^-_t}{P^-_t+r}$.

In steady state, substitute $P^+ = P^- r/(P^-+r)$ into the prediction step. Writing $s = P^-$ and using $a=1$:
\[
s = a^2\,\frac{s\,r}{s+r} + q = \frac{s r}{s+r} + q.
\]
Multiply by $(s+r)$:
\[
s(s+r) = s r + q(s+r) \;\Longrightarrow\; s^2 + s r = s r + q s + q r
\;\Longrightarrow\; s^2 - q s - q r = 0.
\]
With $q=1,r=2$: $s^2 - s - 2 = 0 \Rightarrow (s-2)(s+1)=0$. The positive (admissible) root is $s = 2$.

Therefore
\[
P^- = 2,\qquad P^+ = \frac{P^- r}{P^- + r} = \frac{2\cdot 2}{4} = 1,
\qquad K = \frac{P^-}{P^- + r} = \frac{2}{4} = \boxed{\tfrac12}.
\]
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: $P^- = 2$, $P^+ = 1$, $K = 1/2$.
\item Numerical tolerance: none (all rational); decimal $K=0.5$.
\item Acceptable equivalent forms: $K=0.5$; reporting only $K=1/2$ with correct $P^-,P^+$ is full credit.
\item Deterministic verifier: iterate the Riccati recursion to convergence and compare (see \path{_verify.py}); both roots of $s^2-s-2$ are checked and the negative root $s=-1$ rejected.
\end{itemize}
Mark incorrect: $K = P^+/(P^++r) = 1/3$ (used posterior in the gain); taking the root $s=-1$; or omitting $a^2$ (here $a=1$ hides it, but the algebra must show it).
\end{gradingscheme}

\begin{editornotes}
The benign-looking $a=1$ lets a model skip the $a^2$ term and still get the right number, so the rubric requires the Riccati setup to be structurally correct. The sharper trap is the prior-vs-posterior distinction in the gain definition: $K=P^-/(P^-+r)$, not $P^+/(P^++r)$.
\end{editornotes}

\end{msbproblem}

\end{document}

  • Summary: Hypothesized pattern: (i) using the posterior variance in the gain, `K = P^+/(P^++r) = 1/3`, instead of the prior, `K = P^-/(P^-+r) = 1/2`; (ii) selecting the wrong (negative) Riccati root; (iii) conflating filtered and predicted variances so that all three reported quantities are internally inconsistent. #05

Stationary Rate and Branching Ratio of a Hawkes Process

📄 Submission snapshot — parsed source evidence (private bank, unreleased; do not redistribute)
Synaptic events arrive as a univariate Hawkes process with conditional intensity \[ \lambda(t) = \mu + \sum_{t_i < t} \alpha\, e^{-\beta (t - t_i)}, \] where $\mu = 5~\mathrm{Hz}$ is the baseline (immigrant) rate and the excitation kernel is $\varphi(u) = \alpha e^{-\beta u}$ with $\alpha = 8~\mathrm{s}^{-2}$strong: Deliberately providing $\alpha$ in $\mathrm{s}^{-2}$ (so that $\alpha/\beta$ is dimensionless) sets up the units trap nicely. and $\beta = 10~\mathrm{s}^{-1}$. \begin{enumerate} \item Compute the branching ratio $n = \int_0^\infty \varphi(u)\,du$ and state whether the process is stable. \item Compute the stationary (mean) event rate $\bar\lambda$. \item Compute the mean total number of events in a cluster triggered by a single immigrant (the immigrant plus all of its direct and indirect offspring). \end{enumerate}

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

\begin{enumerate} \item $n = \displaystyle\int_0^\infty \alpha e^{-\beta u}\,du = \frac{\alpha}{\beta} = \frac{8}{10} = 0.8 < 1$, so the process is stable (subcritical). \item Taking expectations of the intensity at stationarity, $\bar\lambda = \mu + n\,\bar\lambda$, hence \[ \bar\lambda = \frac{\mu}{1-n} = \frac{5}{1-0.8} = \frac{5}{0.2} = \boxed{25~\mathrm{Hz}}. \] \item Each event independently triggers $\mathrm{Poisson}(n)$ direct offspring, so the cluster is a Galton--Watson tree with mean offspring $n$. The expected total cluster size (including the root immigrant) is \[ \sum_{k=0}^{\infty} n^k = \frac{1}{1-n} = \frac{1}{0.2} = \boxed{5}. \] \end{enumerate}

Rubric (grading scheme) — how a verifier is meant to grade an answer.

\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: $n = 0.8$ (stable); $\bar\lambda = 25~\mathrm{Hz}$; mean cluster size $= 5$. \item Numerical tolerance: none (all rational). \item Acceptable equivalent forms: $n = 4/5$; $\bar\lambda = 25$; cluster size $=5$. \item Deterministic verifier: arithmetic check of $\alpha/\beta$, $\mu/(1-n)$, $1/(1-n)$ (see \path{_verify.py}). \end{itemize} Mark incorrect: $n = \alpha = 8$ or $n=\alpha\beta$ (kernel not integrated); $\bar\lambda = \mu(1+n) = 9$ or $\mu + n = 5.8$ (wrong fixed point); declaring instability despite $n<1$.

\begin{document}

% Keep this file in sync with the matching problem_05_review.md sidecar.

\problemsetup
 {Stationary Rate and Branching Ratio of a Hawkes Process}
 {Statistics}
 {Self-exciting point processes}
 {Qualifying-exam-plus}
 {Contributor 02 (Dongyu Gong)}
 {\href{mailto:dongyu.gong@yale.edu}{dongyu.gong@yale.edu}}
 {Ready for review}
\problemtags
 {compute / derive}
 {fixed-point reasoning for self-exciting processes, kernel normalization}
 {exact match (rational) plus stability condition}
 {misnormalizing the kernel integral or using $\bar\lambda=\mu(1+n)$ instead of $\mu/(1-n)$}
\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
Synaptic events arrive as a univariate Hawkes process with conditional intensity
\[
\lambda(t) = \mu + \sum_{t_i < t} \alpha\, e^{-\beta (t - t_i)},
\]
where $\mu = 5~\mathrm{Hz}$ is the baseline (immigrant) rate and the excitation kernel is $\varphi(u) = \alpha e^{-\beta u}$ with $\alpha = 8~\mathrm{s}^{-2}$ and $\beta = 10~\mathrm{s}^{-1}$.
\begin{enumerate}
\item Compute the branching ratio $n = \int_0^\infty \varphi(u)\,du$ and state whether the process is stable.
\item Compute the stationary (mean) event rate $\bar\lambda$.
\item Compute the mean total number of events in a cluster triggered by a single immigrant (the immigrant plus all of its direct and indirect offspring).
\end{enumerate}
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
\begin{enumerate}
\item $n = \displaystyle\int_0^\infty \alpha e^{-\beta u}\,du = \frac{\alpha}{\beta} = \frac{8}{10} = 0.8 < 1$, so the process is stable (subcritical).
\item Taking expectations of the intensity at stationarity, $\bar\lambda = \mu + n\,\bar\lambda$, hence
\[
\bar\lambda = \frac{\mu}{1-n} = \frac{5}{1-0.8} = \frac{5}{0.2} = \boxed{25~\mathrm{Hz}}.
\]
\item Each event independently triggers $\mathrm{Poisson}(n)$ direct offspring, so the cluster is a Galton--Watson tree with mean offspring $n$. The expected total cluster size (including the root immigrant) is
\[
\sum_{k=0}^{\infty} n^k = \frac{1}{1-n} = \frac{1}{0.2} = \boxed{5}.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: $n = 0.8$ (stable); $\bar\lambda = 25~\mathrm{Hz}$; mean cluster size $= 5$.
\item Numerical tolerance: none (all rational).
\item Acceptable equivalent forms: $n = 4/5$; $\bar\lambda = 25$; cluster size $=5$.
\item Deterministic verifier: arithmetic check of $\alpha/\beta$, $\mu/(1-n)$, $1/(1-n)$ (see \path{_verify.py}).
\end{itemize}
Mark incorrect: $n = \alpha = 8$ or $n=\alpha\beta$ (kernel not integrated); $\bar\lambda = \mu(1+n) = 9$ or $\mu + n = 5.8$ (wrong fixed point); declaring instability despite $n<1$.
\end{gradingscheme}

\begin{editornotes}
The integral $\int_0^\infty \alpha e^{-\beta u}\,du = \alpha/\beta$ is the only nontrivial step; everything else is a geometric fixed point. The frequent failure is plugging $\alpha$ (with the wrong units, $\mathrm{s}^{-2}$) directly into $\mu/(1-n)$, which also exposes a units-checking weakness.
\end{editornotes}

\end{msbproblem}

\end{document}

## Problem: Stationary Rate and Branching Ratio of a Hawkes Process **Given:** Univariate Hawkes process with conditional intensity λ(t) = μ + Σ_{t_i < t} α exp(−β(t − t_i)) with μ = 5 Hz, α = 8 s⁻², β = 10 s⁻¹. --- ### Part 1: Branching ratio and stability The branching ratio n is the total expected number of direct offspring triggered by a single event, equal to the integral of the excitation kernel: n = ∫₀^∞ φ(u) du = ∫₀^∞ α e^{−βu} du = α · [−(1/β) e^{−βu}]₀^∞ = α · (1/β) = α/β = 8/10 = 0.8 Since n = 0.8 < 1, the process is **subcritical (stable)**. --- ### Part 2: Stationary (mean) event rate At stationarity, the mean intensity is constant. Taking expectations of λ(t) using Campbell's theorem and the stationarity of the process: λ̄ = μ + λ̄ · ∫₀^∞ φ(u) du = μ + n · λ̄ This is a fixed-point equation. Solving: λ̄ − n λ̄ = μ λ̄ (1 − n) = μ λ̄ = μ/(1 − n) = 5/(1 − 0.8) = 5/0.2 = **25 Hz** Note: a common error is using λ̄ = μ(1 + n) = 9 or λ̄ = μ + n = 5.8, which would arise from not correctly setting up the fixed-point equation. --- ### Part 3: Mean cluster size In the cluster (immigration–birth) representation of the Hawkes process, the process is a superposition of clusters. Each immigrant independently starts a cluster. Within a cluster, each event spawns Poisson(n) direct offspring, forming a Galton–Watson branching process with mean offspring number n. Starting from one immigrant, the expected total cluster size (including the immigrant itself and all generations of offspring) is the sum of expected generation sizes: E[cluster size] = Σ_{k=0}^∞ n^k = 1/(1 − n) = 1/(1 − 0.8) = 1/0.2 = **5** --- ### Summary of answers 1. n = α/β = 8/10 = 0.8 < 1 → process is stable (subcritical) 2. λ̄ = μ/(1 − n) = 5/0.2 = 25 Hz 3. Mean cluster size = 1/(1 − n) = 1/0.2 = 5

Problem: Univariate Hawkes process with intensity λ(t) = μ + Σ_{t_i<t} α e^{−β(t−t_i)}, with μ = 5 Hz, kernel φ(u) = α e^{−βu}, α = 8 s^{−2}, β = 10 s^{−1}. Part 1 — Branching ratio and stability. The branching ratio is the integral of the excitation kernel: n = ∫₀^∞ φ(u) du = ∫₀^∞ α e^{−βu} du = α/β = 8/10 = 0.8. Since n = 0.8 < 1, the process is stable (subcritical). (Units check: α has units s^{−2}, β has units s^{−1}, so α/β is dimensionless, as a branching ratio must be — confirming we must integrate the kernel rather than plug α in directly.) Part 2 — Stationary mean rate. At stationarity the mean intensity λ̄ = E[λ(t)] is constant. Taking expectations of the intensity equation and using that the expected contribution of the self-exciting term equals (∫φ)·λ̄ = n·λ̄ (Campbell-type / stationarity argument), we get the fixed-point equation: λ̄ = μ + n·λ̄ ⇒ λ̄(1 − n) = μ ⇒ λ̄ = μ/(1 − n). Numerically: λ̄ = 5/(1 − 0.8) = 5/0.2 = 25 Hz. Part 3 — Mean total cluster size. The cluster generated by one immigrant is a Galton–Watson branching tree: each event independently produces a Poisson(n) number of direct offspring (the standard immigrant–offspring representation of a Hawkes process), so the mean number of direct offspring per event is n. Summing over all generations (generation k contributes mean n^k descendants), the expected total number of events in the cluster, including the root immigrant, is: E[cluster size] = Σ_{k=0}^∞ n^k = 1/(1 − n) = 1/(1 − 0.8) = 1/0.2 = 5. Final answers: 1. n = α/β = 0.8 < 1 ⇒ stable (subcritical). 2. λ̄ = μ/(1 − n) = 25 Hz. 3. Mean total cluster size = 1/(1 − n) = 5.

Your failure-mode summary:

  • Observed failure mode: misnormalizing the kernel integral, or using rate = mu(1+n) instead of mu/(1-n)
  • Summary: Hypothesized pattern: (i) failing to integrate the kernel and using `n = alpha = 8` (which also ignores the units mismatch: alpha has units s^-2), giving an absurd super-critical value; (ii) the fixed-point error `lambda_bar = mu(1+n) = 9` or `mu + n = 5.8`; (iii) reporting cluster size as `n/(1-n) = 4` (expected offspring excluding the root) when the question asks for the total including the immigrant. The units check (alpha = 8 s^-2 vs. n dimensionless) is a strong falsifier.
