#06

Mutual Information of a Correlated Two-Neuron Gaussian Channel

📄 Submission snapshot — parsed source evidence (private bank, unreleased; do not redistribute)
A scalar stimulus $s \sim \mathcal{N}(0, \sigma_s^2)$ with $\sigma_s^2 = 1$ drives two neurons whose responses are \[ \mathbf{r} = \mathbf{g}\, s + \boldsymbol{\eta}, \qquad \boldsymbol{\eta} \sim \mathcal{N}\!\big(\mathbf{0}, \Sigma\big), \quad \Sigma = \sigma^2\begin{pmatrix} 1 & \rho \\ \rho & 1 \end{pmatrix}, \] with gain vector $\mathbf{g} = (1,1)^{\top}$, $\sigma^2 = 1$, and noise correlation $\rho \in (-1,1)$. The noise $\boldsymbol\eta$ is independent of $s$. \begin{enumerate} \item Derive the mutual information $I(s;\mathbf{r})$ in bits as a function of $\rho$. \item Evaluate $I$ at $\rho = -\tfrac13$ and at $\rho = 0$nit: Two evaluation points are enough for the grader; if you want to raise difficulty, swap one for a less round value (e.g. $\rho = -1/2$) so the answer isn't a clean integer bit. (independent noise). \item Based on the sign of $dI/d\rho$ for this $\mathbf{g}$, state whether positive or negative noise correlations are beneficial herepraise: Good — explicitly scoped to 'for this g', which forecloses the over-general answer that's the interesting failure mode., and give $\lim_{\rho\to -1^+} I$suggestion: Consider asking the solver to also briefly justify the divergence (e.g. signal aligns with vanishing noise eigenvalue) — turns a one-token answer into a probe of understanding without changing the verifier.. \end{enumerate}

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

For a Gaussian channel $\mathbf r = \mathbf g s + \boldsymbol\eta$ with $s\sim\mathcal N(0,\sigma_s^2)$, \[ I(s;\mathbf r) = \tfrac12 \log_2\!\big(1 + \sigma_s^2\, \mathbf g^{\top}\Sigma^{-1}\mathbf g\big). \] For $\mathbf g=(1,1)^\top$ and $\Sigma=\sigma^2\big(\begin{smallmatrix}1&\rho\\\rho&1\end{smallmatrix}\big)$, \[ \mathbf g^{\top}\Sigma^{-1}\mathbf g = \frac{1}{\sigma^2(1-\rho^2)}(1 - \rho - \rho + 1) = \frac{2(1-\rho)}{\sigma^2(1-\rho^2)} = \frac{2}{\sigma^2(1+\rho)}. \] With $\sigma^2=\sigma_s^2=1$, \[ I(\rho) = \tfrac12\log_2\!\Big(1 + \tfrac{2}{1+\rho}\Big). \] \begin{itemize} \item $\rho=-\tfrac13$: $\tfrac{2}{1+\rho}=\tfrac{2}{2/3}=3$, so $I=\tfrac12\log_2(1+3)=\tfrac12\log_2 4 = \boxed{1~\text{bit}}$. \item $\rho=0$: $\tfrac{2}{1}=2$, so $I=\tfrac12\log_2 3 \approx \boxed{0.7925~\text{bits}}$. \end{itemize} Since $I(\rho)=\tfrac12\log_2\!\big(1+\tfrac{2}{1+\rho}\big)$ is strictly \emph{decreasing} in $\rho$, \emph{negative} correlations are beneficial for this gain vector (the signal $\propto(1,1)$ aligns with the high-variance noise mode when $\rho>0$, and with the low-variance mode when $\rho<0$). As $\rho\to-1^+$, $\tfrac{2}{1+\rho}\to+\infty$ and $I\to+\infty$.

Rubric (grading scheme) — how a verifier is meant to grade an answer.

\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer plus required comparison.} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: $I(-1/3) = 1$ bit; $I(0) = \tfrac12\log_2 3 \approx 0.7925$ bits; negative correlations help; $\lim_{\rho\to-1^+}I=+\infty$. \item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.002$ bits on $0.7925$. \item Acceptable equivalent forms: $I(\rho)=\tfrac12\log_2\!\big(1+\tfrac{2}{1+\rho}\big)$; bits required (answers in nats must be explicitly converted). \item Deterministic verifier: evaluate the closed form at the two $\rho$ values (see \path{_verify.py}). \end{itemize} Mark incorrect: $I(-1/3)=1.386$ (used $\ln$ not $\log_2$); answers without the factor $1/2$; claiming positive correlations are beneficial; or computing $\mathbf g^\top\Sigma^{-1}\mathbf g$ as $2/(1-\rho)$.
raw.tex source (provenance — verbatim, unparsed)

The full unparsed .tex as it was on master at review time (content sha d883a620) — kept for provenance only; the parsed blocks above are the evidence to read.

\documentclass[../../../main.tex]{subfiles}
\begin{document}

% Keep this file in sync with the matching problem_06_review.md sidecar.

\problemsetup
 {Mutual Information of a Correlated Two-Neuron Gaussian Channel}
 {Statistics / Mathematics}
 {Information theory / population coding}
 {Qualifying-exam-plus}
 {Contributor 02 (Dongyu Gong)}
 {\href{mailto:dongyu.gong@yale.edu}{dongyu.gong@yale.edu}}
 {Ready for review}
\problemtags
 {compute / derive}
 {Gaussian-channel information, the (counterintuitive) sign of noise correlations}
 {exact match (bits) plus comparison}
 {natural-log vs.\ $\log_2$, dropped factor $1/2$, or assuming positive correlations always help}
\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
A scalar stimulus $s \sim \mathcal{N}(0, \sigma_s^2)$ with $\sigma_s^2 = 1$ drives two neurons whose responses are
\[
\mathbf{r} = \mathbf{g}\, s + \boldsymbol{\eta}, \qquad
\boldsymbol{\eta} \sim \mathcal{N}\!\big(\mathbf{0}, \Sigma\big), \quad
\Sigma = \sigma^2\begin{pmatrix} 1 & \rho \\ \rho & 1 \end{pmatrix},
\]
with gain vector $\mathbf{g} = (1,1)^{\top}$, $\sigma^2 = 1$, and noise correlation $\rho \in (-1,1)$. The noise $\boldsymbol\eta$ is independent of $s$.
\begin{enumerate}
\item Derive the mutual information $I(s;\mathbf{r})$ in bits as a function of $\rho$.
\item Evaluate $I$ at $\rho = -\tfrac13$ and at $\rho = 0$ (independent noise).
\item Based on the sign of $dI/d\rho$ for this $\mathbf{g}$, state whether positive or negative noise correlations are beneficial here, and give $\lim_{\rho\to -1^+} I$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
For a Gaussian channel $\mathbf r = \mathbf g s + \boldsymbol\eta$ with $s\sim\mathcal N(0,\sigma_s^2)$,
\[
I(s;\mathbf r) = \tfrac12 \log_2\!\big(1 + \sigma_s^2\, \mathbf g^{\top}\Sigma^{-1}\mathbf g\big).
\]
For $\mathbf g=(1,1)^\top$ and $\Sigma=\sigma^2\big(\begin{smallmatrix}1&\rho\\\rho&1\end{smallmatrix}\big)$,
\[
\mathbf g^{\top}\Sigma^{-1}\mathbf g
= \frac{1}{\sigma^2(1-\rho^2)}(1 - \rho - \rho + 1)
= \frac{2(1-\rho)}{\sigma^2(1-\rho^2)}
= \frac{2}{\sigma^2(1+\rho)}.
\]
With $\sigma^2=\sigma_s^2=1$,
\[
I(\rho) = \tfrac12\log_2\!\Big(1 + \tfrac{2}{1+\rho}\Big).
\]
\begin{itemize}
\item $\rho=-\tfrac13$: $\tfrac{2}{1+\rho}=\tfrac{2}{2/3}=3$, so $I=\tfrac12\log_2(1+3)=\tfrac12\log_2 4 = \boxed{1~\text{bit}}$.
\item $\rho=0$: $\tfrac{2}{1}=2$, so $I=\tfrac12\log_2 3 \approx \boxed{0.7925~\text{bits}}$.
\end{itemize}
Since $I(\rho)=\tfrac12\log_2\!\big(1+\tfrac{2}{1+\rho}\big)$ is strictly \emph{decreasing} in $\rho$, \emph{negative} correlations are beneficial for this gain vector (the signal $\propto(1,1)$ aligns with the high-variance noise mode when $\rho>0$, and with the low-variance mode when $\rho<0$). As $\rho\to-1^+$, $\tfrac{2}{1+\rho}\to+\infty$ and $I\to+\infty$.
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer plus required comparison.}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: $I(-1/3) = 1$ bit; $I(0) = \tfrac12\log_2 3 \approx 0.7925$ bits; negative correlations help; $\lim_{\rho\to-1^+}I=+\infty$.
\item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.002$ bits on $0.7925$.
\item Acceptable equivalent forms: $I(\rho)=\tfrac12\log_2\!\big(1+\tfrac{2}{1+\rho}\big)$; bits required (answers in nats must be explicitly converted).
\item Deterministic verifier: evaluate the closed form at the two $\rho$ values (see \path{_verify.py}).
\end{itemize}
Mark incorrect: $I(-1/3)=1.386$ (used $\ln$ not $\log_2$); answers without the factor $1/2$; claiming positive correlations are beneficial; or computing $\mathbf g^\top\Sigma^{-1}\mathbf g$ as $2/(1-\rho)$.
\end{gradingscheme}

\begin{editornotes}
The interesting failure is conceptual: many responses assert that positive noise correlations are harmful and negative ones helpful \emph{in general}, or vice versa, whereas the sign of the effect depends on the alignment of $\mathbf g$ with the noise eigenvectors. Here $\mathbf g\propto(1,1)$ is the $1+\rho$ eigen-direction, so the dependence is monotone decreasing.
\end{editornotes}

\end{msbproblem}

\end{document}

#07

Participation Ratio of an Exponentially Correlated Covariance

📄 Submission snapshot — parsed source evidence (private bank, unreleased; do not redistribute)
The activity of $N=4$ neuronssuggestion: For a Qualifying-exam-plus target, N=4 makes brute-force diagonalization tractable. Consider general N (closed form via geometric sum) to raise the cognitive load. has covariance matrix with exponentially decaying correlations, \[ C_{ij} = \rho^{\,|i-j|}, \qquad i,j \in \{1,2,3,4\}, \quad \rho = \tfrac12, \] (unit variances on the diagonal). The \emph{participation ratio} (a standard linear measure of effective dimensionality) is \[ \mathrm{PR}(C) = \frac{\big(\sum_i \lambda_i\big)^2}{\sum_i \lambda_i^2} = \frac{(\operatorname{tr} C)^2}{\operatorname{tr}(C^2)}, \] where $\lambda_i$ are the eigenvalues of $C$. Compute $\mathrm{PR}(C)$ exactly (as a reduced fraction) and give a 4-decimal value.

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

Use $\operatorname{tr}(C^2)=\sum_{i,j}C_{ij}^2$ (valid because $C$ is symmetric, so $\operatorname{tr}(C^2)=\sum_{i,j}C_{ij}C_{ji}=\sum_{i,j}C_{ij}^2=\sum_i\lambda_i^2$). No diagonalization is needed. $\operatorname{tr} C = 4$, so $(\operatorname{tr} C)^2 = 16$. With $C_{ij}^2 = (\rho^2)^{|i-j|} = (1/4)^{|i-j|}$, count ordered pairs by lag $|i-j|=d$ in a length-4 chain ($2(N-d)$ ordered pairs for $d\ge1$, $N$ for $d=0$): \[ \operatorname{tr}(C^2) = \underbrace{4\cdot 1}_{d=0} + \underbrace{6\cdot \tfrac14}_{d=1} + \underbrace{4\cdot \tfrac1{16}}_{d=2} + \underbrace{2\cdot \tfrac1{64}}_{d=3} = 4 + \tfrac32 + \tfrac14 + \tfrac1{32} = \frac{185}{32}. \] Therefore \[ \mathrm{PR}(C) = \frac{16}{185/32} = \frac{512}{185} \approx \boxed{2.7676}. \] (Sanity: $1 \le \mathrm{PR} \le N = 4$, and $2.77$ is sensible for moderately correlated unit-variance channels.)

Rubric (grading scheme) — how a verifier is meant to grade an answer.

\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: $\mathrm{PR} = 512/185 \approx 2.7676$. \item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.001$ on the decimal. \item Acceptable equivalent forms: $512/185$; $16/(185/32)$. \item Deterministic verifier: compare to $512/185$; cross-check by diagonalizing $C$ numerically and summing $\lambda_i,\lambda_i^2$ (see \path{_verify.py}). \end{itemize} Mark incorrect: $\operatorname{tr}(C^2)=4$ (used only the diagonal); off-diagonal lag counts that drop the factor $2$ (treating unordered pairs); or eigenvalue computations that misreport $\sum\lambda_i^2$.

\problemsetup
 {Participation Ratio of an Exponentially Correlated Covariance}
 {Mathematics / Statistics}
 {Linear algebra / dimensionality of neural activity}
 {Qualifying-exam-plus}
 {Contributor 02 (Dongyu Gong)}
 {\href{mailto:dongyu.gong@yale.edu}{dongyu.gong@yale.edu}}
 {Ready for review}
\problemtags
 {compute}
 {trace identities, computing $\operatorname{tr}(C^2)$ as a sum of squared entries}
 {exact match (reduced fraction) plus numerical tolerance}
 {summing only diagonal squares, double-counting off-diagonals, or diagonalizing unnecessarily and slipping}
\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
The activity of $N=4$ neurons has covariance matrix with exponentially decaying correlations,
\[
C_{ij} = \rho^{\,|i-j|}, \qquad i,j \in \{1,2,3,4\}, \quad \rho = \tfrac12,
\]
(unit variances on the diagonal). The \emph{participation ratio} (a standard linear measure of effective dimensionality) is
\[
\mathrm{PR}(C) = \frac{\big(\sum_i \lambda_i\big)^2}{\sum_i \lambda_i^2} = \frac{(\operatorname{tr} C)^2}{\operatorname{tr}(C^2)},
\]
where $\lambda_i$ are the eigenvalues of $C$.

Compute $\mathrm{PR}(C)$ exactly (as a reduced fraction) and give a 4-decimal value.
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
Use $\operatorname{tr}(C^2)=\sum_{i,j}C_{ij}^2$ (valid because $C$ is symmetric, so $\operatorname{tr}(C^2)=\sum_{i,j}C_{ij}C_{ji}=\sum_{i,j}C_{ij}^2=\sum_i\lambda_i^2$). No diagonalization is needed.

$\operatorname{tr} C = 4$, so $(\operatorname{tr} C)^2 = 16$.

With $C_{ij}^2 = (\rho^2)^{|i-j|} = (1/4)^{|i-j|}$, count ordered pairs by lag $|i-j|=d$ in a length-4 chain ($2(N-d)$ ordered pairs for $d\ge1$, $N$ for $d=0$):
\[
\operatorname{tr}(C^2) = \underbrace{4\cdot 1}_{d=0} + \underbrace{6\cdot \tfrac14}_{d=1} + \underbrace{4\cdot \tfrac1{16}}_{d=2} + \underbrace{2\cdot \tfrac1{64}}_{d=3}
= 4 + \tfrac32 + \tfrac14 + \tfrac1{32} = \frac{185}{32}.
\]
Therefore
\[
\mathrm{PR}(C) = \frac{16}{185/32} = \frac{512}{185} \approx \boxed{2.7676}.
\]
(Sanity: $1 \le \mathrm{PR} \le N = 4$, and $2.77$ is sensible for moderately correlated unit-variance channels.)
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: $\mathrm{PR} = 512/185 \approx 2.7676$.
\item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.001$ on the decimal.
\item Acceptable equivalent forms: $512/185$; $16/(185/32)$.
\item Deterministic verifier: compare to $512/185$; cross-check by diagonalizing $C$ numerically and summing $\lambda_i,\lambda_i^2$ (see \path{_verify.py}).
\end{itemize}
Mark incorrect: $\operatorname{tr}(C^2)=4$ (used only the diagonal); off-diagonal lag counts that drop the factor $2$ (treating unordered pairs); or eigenvalue computations that misreport $\sum\lambda_i^2$.
\end{gradingscheme}

\begin{editornotes}
The deterministic core is the identity $\operatorname{tr}(C^2)=\sum_{i,j}C_{ij}^2$. Models that diagonalize the $4\times4$ matrix by hand often make an arithmetic slip; the trace-identity route is exact and short, but requires remembering to count both orderings of each off-diagonal lag.
\end{editornotes}

\end{msbproblem}

\end{document}

## Participation Ratio of an Exponentially Correlated Covariance ### Setup We have N=4 neurons with covariance matrix C_ij = rho^|i-j|, rho = 1/2: ``` C = [[1, 1/2, 1/4, 1/8 ], [1/2, 1, 1/2, 1/4 ], [1/4, 1/2, 1, 1/2 ], [1/8, 1/4, 1/2, 1 ]] ``` The participation ratio is PR(C) = (tr C)^2 / tr(C^2). ### Step 1: Compute tr(C) tr(C) = 1+1+1+1 = 4, so (tr C)^2 = 16. ### Step 2: Compute tr(C^2) without diagonalizing Key identity: since C is symmetric, tr(C^2) = sum_{i,j} C_ij^2. With C_ij^2 = (rho^2)^|i-j| = (1/4)^|i-j|, group by lag d = |i-j|. For a length-N chain, the number of ordered pairs at lag d is N for d=0 and 2(N-d) for d >= 1. - d=0: 4 pairs × 1 = 4 - d=1: 6 pairs × (1/4) = 6/4 = 3/2 - d=2: 4 pairs × (1/16) = 4/16 = 1/4 - d=3: 2 pairs × (1/64) = 2/64 = 1/32 tr(C^2) = 4 + 3/2 + 1/4 + 1/32 = 128/32 + 48/32 + 8/32 + 1/32 = 185/32 ### Step 3: Compute PR(C) PR(C) = 16 / (185/32) = 16 × 32/185 = 512/185 Checking reduced form: 512 = 2^9 and 185 = 5 × 37 share no common factors, so 512/185 is fully reduced. ### Final Answer PR(C) = 512/185 ≈ 2.7676 Sanity check: 1 ≤ PR ≤ N = 4. The value 2.77 is sensible for moderately correlated unit-variance channels (if all were uncorrelated, PR = 4; if perfectly correlated, PR = 1).

PR = 512/185. Work: C is 4×4 with C_ij = (1/2)^|i-j|. Diagonal = 1. - tr C = 4, so (tr C)^2 = 16. - tr(C^2) = Σ_ij C_ij^2 (C symmetric). Counting by |i-j|: |i-j|=0: 4 entries × 1 = 4 |i-j|=1: 6 entries × (1/4) = 3/2 |i-j|=2: 4 entries × (1/16) = 1/4 |i-j|=3: 2 entries × (1/64) = 1/32 Sum = 128/32 + 48/32 + 8/32 + 1/32 = 185/32. - PR = 16 / (185/32) = 512/185. 512 = 2^9 and 185 = 5·37 share no common factors, so 512/185 is fully reduced (≈ 2.7676).
claude-opus-4-8 · free-subagent (1 runs — every captured sample for this model × setup; linked from the results matrix above)

free-subagent k0PR(C) = 512/185 ≈ 2.7676 ✓ pass0/0—
trace
response
We compute the participation ratio PR(C) = (tr C)² / tr(C²) for the N=4 covariance matrix C with C_ij = ρ^|i-j|, ρ = 1/2. Approach: Use the identity tr(C²) = Σ_{i,j} C_ij² (valid since C is symmetric: tr(C²) = Σ_{i,j} C_ij C_ji = Σ_{i,j} C_ij²). No diagonalization needed. Numerator: The diagonal entries are C_ii = ρ⁰ = 1, so tr C = 4 and (tr C)² = 16. Denominator: C_ij² = (ρ²)^|i-j| = (1/4)^|i-j|. Count ordered pairs (i,j) by lag d = |i-j| in a length-4 chain. For d ≥ 1 there are 2(N-d) ordered pairs; for d = 0 there are N pairs. - d=0: 4 pairs × 1 = 4 - d=1: 6 pairs × 1/4 = 3/2 - d=2: 4 pairs × 1/16 = 1/4 - d=3: 2 pairs × 1/64 = 1/32 tr(C²) = 4 + 3/2 + 1/4 + 1/32 = 128/32 + 48/32 + 8/32 + 1/32 = 185/32. Participation ratio: PR(C) = 16 / (185/32) = 16 · 32 / 185 = 512/185 ≈ 2.7676. Sanity check: 1 ≤ PR ≤ N = 4, and 2.77 is reasonable for moderately correlated unit-variance channels. Final answer: PR(C) = 512/185 ≈ 2.7676.
🗒️ Contributor’s own notes / pasted responses

Your own review sidecar — the responses you collected and your own failure-mode read. Shown verbatim, as your words (distinct from the bot review and the model sweep below).

Your failure-mode summary:

  • Observed failure mode: summing only diagonal squares, double-counting off-diagonals, or diagonalizing and slipping
  • Summary: Hypothesized pattern: (i) computing `tr(C^2) = tr(C) = 4` by using only diagonal entries (forgetting that off-diagonal entries contribute to C^2); (ii) counting each off-diagonal lag once instead of twice (unordered vs. ordered pairs), giving a too-small tr(C^2); (iii) attempting an explicit hand diagonalization of the 4x4 matrix and making an arithmetic slip. The trace-identity route is short and exact but hinges on the factor-of-2 lag counting.

"""Deterministic verifier — contributor_02 / problem_07.
Participation Ratio of an Exponentially Correlated Covariance. Hand-authored (Claude Code, autonomous; via @yulinl2).

import re
from fractions import Fraction

_MARKERS = [
 r"final\s+answer\s*[:=]?", r"the\s+(?:exact\s+)?answer\s+is\s*[:=]?",
 r"answer\s*[:=]", r"the\s+rate\s+is\s*[:=]?", r"therefore[,:]?",
 r"thus[,:]?", r"hence[,:]?",
]
_MARKER_RE = re.compile("|".join(_MARKERS), re.IGNORECASE)

def _boxed_inner(s):
 """Brace-balanced contents of every \\boxed{...} (handles nested \\frac)."""
 out, i = [], 0
 while True:
 j = s.find("boxed", i)
 if j < 0:
 break
 k = s.find("{", j)
 if k < 0:
 break
 depth, m = 0, k
 while m < len(s):
 if s[m] == "{":
 depth += 1
 elif s[m] == "}":
 depth -= 1
 if depth == 0:
 break
 m += 1
 out.append(s[k + 1:m])
 i = m + 1
 return out

def _normalize(s):
 """LaTeX -> plain so rationals survive: \\frac{a}{b} -> a/b, keep %.
 (c09 fractions are always integer/integer, so the flat a/b form is exact;
 a compound numerator would need parens, not present here.)"""
 # capture an optional leading minus OUTSIDE the frac (-\frac{a}{b} -> -a/b):
 # a detached "-" would otherwise be split off and the component read positive.
 s = re.sub(r"(-?)\s*\\[dt]?frac\s*\{([^{}]*)\}\s*\{([^{}]*)\}", r" \1\2/\3 ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\(left|right|!|,|;|:|quad|qquad)", " ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\text\s*\{[^{}]*\}", " ", s)
 s = s.replace(r"\%", "%").replace(r"\$", " ").replace("$", " ")
 s = s.replace("{,}", "") # 225{,}000 -> 225000
 s = s.replace("\\", " ").replace("{", " ").replace("}", " ")
 # thousands separator ONLY when strictly adjacent (1,250 -> 1250); never join
 # across a space (a vector's "1907, 379" must stay two numbers).
 s = re.sub(r"(\d),(\d{3})(?!\d)", r"\1\2", s)
 return s

# token: percent | fraction a/b | decimal | integer (left-to-right, non-overlap)
_TOK = re.compile(
 r"(?P<pct>-?\d+(?:\.\d+)?)\s*%"
 r"|(?P<n>-?\d+)\s*/\s*(?P<d>-?\d+)"
 r"|(?P<dec>-?\d+\.\d+)"
 r"|(?P<int>-?\d+)")

def _rationals(zone):
 """Ordered list of Fraction values committed in `zone` (already a string)."""
 out = []
 for m in _TOK.finditer(_normalize(zone)):
 try:
 if m.group("pct") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("pct")) / 100)
 elif m.group("n") is not None:
 d = int(m.group("d"))
 if d == 0:
 continue
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("n")), d))
 elif m.group("dec") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("dec")))
 else:
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("int"))))
 except (ValueError, ZeroDivisionError):
 continue
 return out

def _final_zone(response):
 """The text of the model's committed final answer (most -> least reliable):
 boxed contents; else the last marker line's tail; else the last line."""
 if not response or not response.strip:
 return ""
 boxed = _boxed_inner(response)
 if boxed:
 return " ".join(boxed)
 lines = [ln for ln in response.splitlines if ln.strip]
 # last marker line that actually carries a number (weak discourse markers
 # like "therefore/thus/hence" otherwise hijack the zone to a number-less
 # trailing comment, burying the real commit on the line above).
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 m = _MARKER_RE.search(ln)
 if m and any(c.isdigit for c in ln[m.start:]):
 return ln[m.start:]
 # else the last line that carries a number
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 if any(c.isdigit for c in ln):
 return ln
 return lines[-1] if lines else ""

def _committed_rational(zone):
 """The single COMMITTED final rational in `zone`: the value after the LAST
 '=' if one is present (so an arithmetic line like '105 - 105 = 0' commits 0,
 NOT the intermediate 105 — the loose 'oracle appears anywhere' false-positive
 the cold-read found), else the last rational. Returns (value|None, all)."""
 cands = _rationals(zone)
 if not cands:
 return None, []
 norm = _normalize(zone)
 if "=" in norm:
 tail = _rationals(norm.rsplit("=", 1)[1])
 if tail:
 return tail[-1], cands
 # no '=': the committed value is the FIRST rational (right after the marker /
 # at the head of the line) — a trailing parenthetical like "0 (note 105)" or
 # "253 (> 200)" must not steal the verdict.
 return cands[0], cands

def match_exact(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction. The COMMITTED final must equal it EXACTLY (no tolerance
 — rounded-only decimals are insufficient per the gradingscheme). Grades the
 committed value, not any number in the zone (no substring/intermediate pass)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 if committed == oracle:
 return True, "committed %s == oracle %s" % (committed, oracle)
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (cands %s)" % (
 committed, oracle, [str(c) for c in cands])

def match_vector(response, oracle):
 """oracle: list[Fraction]. Every component must appear in the final zone."""
 cands = set(_rationals(_final_zone(response)))
 missing = [str(o) for o in oracle if o not in cands]
 if not missing:
 return True, "all %d components present" % len(oracle)
 return False, "missing/incorrect components: %s" % missing

def match_rate(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction rate (e.g. 39/125). Accept the exact fraction, the
 decimal (tol 1e-4, the percent form is inherently rounded), or the percent
 (bare integer like 31.2 read as 31.2%)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 r = oracle
 rf = float(r)
 cf = float(committed)
 if committed == r:
 return True, "exact %s == oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf) < 1e-4:
 return True, "decimal %s ~= oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf * 100) < 1e-2:
 return True, "percent %s ~= oracle %s%%" % (committed, float(r * 100))
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (=%.6g; cands %s)" % (
 committed, r, rf, [str(c) for c in cands])


def grade(response: str, context: dict) -> dict:
 """Participation Ratio of an Exponentially Correlated Covariance

 Oracle: Fraction(512,185) (author answerkey, sympy-reconfirmed). Accept the EXACT
 reduced fraction OR a 4-decimal value (tol 5e-5); a coarsely-rounded
 decimal alone is insufficient (gradingscheme asks for both forms)."""
 oracle = Fraction(512,185)
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return {"pass": False, "score": 0.0, "notes": "no rational final answer extracted"}
 if committed == oracle or abs(float(committed) - float(oracle)) < 5e-5:
 return {"pass": True, "score": 1.0,
 "notes": "committed %s matches oracle %s" % (committed, oracle)}
 return {"pass": False, "score": 0.0,
 "notes": "committed %s != oracle %s (cands %s)" % (committed, oracle, [str(c) for c in cands])}


if __name__ == '__main__':
 import sys, json
 data = json.load(sys.stdin)
 out = grade(response=data['response'], context=data['context'])
 print(json.dumps(out))

#08

Conditional Spike Timing: Order Statistics of a Poisson Process

📄 Submission snapshot — parsed source evidence (private bank, unreleased; do not redistribute)
A neuron fires according to a homogeneous Poisson process of rate $\lambda$ on the window $[0,T]$. Condition on the event that \emph{exactly three} spikes occurred in $[0,T]$, with ordered spike times $0 < S_{(1)} < S_{(2)} < S_{(3)} < T$. \begin{enumerate} \item State the conditional joint distribution of $(S_{(1)},S_{(2)},S_{(3)})$ given the count is $3$. \item Compute the probability that the \emph{middle} spike $S_{(2)}$ falls in the central third $[\,T/3,\ 2T/3\,]$. Give the exact reduced fraction and a 4-decimal value. \end{enumerate} (The answer should not depend on $\lambda$ or $T$.strong: Helpful hint that nudges the solver toward the right invariance without giving the trick away.)

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

\textbf{(1)} Given exactly $n$ spikes in $[0,T]$, the conditional joint law of the unordered spike times is that of $n$ i.i.d.\ $\mathrm{Uniform}[0,T]$ points; equivalently the ordered times are the order statistics of $n$ i.i.d.\ uniforms. This is independent of $\lambda$. \textbf{(2)} Let $U_{(2)}$ be the median of three i.i.d.\ $\mathrm{Uniform}[0,1]$ variables (rescale $T=1$). Then $U_{(2)}\sim\mathrm{Beta}(2,2)$ with density $f(x)=6x(1-x)$ on $[0,1]$. Hence \[ \Pr\!\Big(\tfrac13 \le U_{(2)} \le \tfrac23\Big) = \int_{1/3}^{2/3} 6x(1-x)\,dx = \big[\,3x^2 - 2x^3\,\big]_{1/3}^{2/3}. \] At $x=2/3$: $3\cdot\tfrac49 - 2\cdot\tfrac{8}{27} = \tfrac{12}{9}-\tfrac{16}{27} = \tfrac{20}{27}$. At $x=1/3$: $3\cdot\tfrac19 - 2\cdot\tfrac1{27} = \tfrac39-\tfrac2{27} = \tfrac{7}{27}$. \[ \Pr = \frac{20}{27}-\frac{7}{27} = \boxed{\dfrac{13}{27}} \approx 0.4815. \]

Rubric (grading scheme) — how a verifier is meant to grade an answer.

\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: $13/27 \approx 0.4815$; the conditional law is uniform order statistics, $S_{(2)}/T\sim\mathrm{Beta}(2,2)$. \item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.001$ on the decimal. \item Acceptable equivalent forms: $13/27$; the integral $\int_{1/3}^{2/3}6x(1-x)\,dx$. \item Deterministic verifier: closed-form integral plus Monte-Carlo of $\operatorname{median}$ of three uniforms giving $\approx 0.482$ (see \path{_verify.py}). \end{itemize} Mark incorrect: answer $1/3$ (treated $S_{(2)}$ as a single uniform, ignoring that it is the median); using exponential-ISI reasoning conditioned on the count; or a Beta$(2,2)$ density with the wrong normalizing constant.
raw.tex source (provenance — verbatim, unparsed)

\documentclass[../../../main.tex]{subfiles}
\begin{document}

% Keep this file in sync with the matching problem_08_review.md sidecar.

\problemsetup
 {Conditional Spike Timing: Order Statistics of a Poisson Process}
 {Statistics}
 {Probability / order statistics}
 {Qualifying-exam-plus}
 {Contributor 02 (Dongyu Gong)}
 {\href{mailto:dongyu.gong@yale.edu}{dongyu.gong@yale.edu}}
 {Ready for review}
\problemtags
 {compute / derive}
 {conditional structure of a Poisson process, order statistics vs.\ marginal uniform}
 {exact match (reduced fraction) plus numerical tolerance}
 {ignoring that the middle spike is the median (Beta$(2,2)$), treating it as a single uniform}
\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
A neuron fires according to a homogeneous Poisson process of rate $\lambda$ on the window $[0,T]$. Condition on the event that \emph{exactly three} spikes occurred in $[0,T]$, with ordered spike times $0 < S_{(1)} < S_{(2)} < S_{(3)} < T$.
\begin{enumerate}
\item State the conditional joint distribution of $(S_{(1)},S_{(2)},S_{(3)})$ given the count is $3$.
\item Compute the probability that the \emph{middle} spike $S_{(2)}$ falls in the central third $[\,T/3,\ 2T/3\,]$. Give the exact reduced fraction and a 4-decimal value.
\end{enumerate}
(The answer should not depend on $\lambda$ or $T$.)
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
\textbf{(1)} Given exactly $n$ spikes in $[0,T]$, the conditional joint law of the unordered spike times is that of $n$ i.i.d.\ $\mathrm{Uniform}[0,T]$ points; equivalently the ordered times are the order statistics of $n$ i.i.d.\ uniforms. This is independent of $\lambda$.

\textbf{(2)} Let $U_{(2)}$ be the median of three i.i.d.\ $\mathrm{Uniform}[0,1]$ variables (rescale $T=1$). Then $U_{(2)}\sim\mathrm{Beta}(2,2)$ with density $f(x)=6x(1-x)$ on $[0,1]$. Hence
\[
\Pr\!\Big(\tfrac13 \le U_{(2)} \le \tfrac23\Big)
= \int_{1/3}^{2/3} 6x(1-x)\,dx
= \big[\,3x^2 - 2x^3\,\big]_{1/3}^{2/3}.
\]
At $x=2/3$: $3\cdot\tfrac49 - 2\cdot\tfrac{8}{27} = \tfrac{12}{9}-\tfrac{16}{27} = \tfrac{20}{27}$.
At $x=1/3$: $3\cdot\tfrac19 - 2\cdot\tfrac1{27} = \tfrac39-\tfrac2{27} = \tfrac{7}{27}$.
\[
\Pr = \frac{20}{27}-\frac{7}{27} = \boxed{\dfrac{13}{27}} \approx 0.4815.
\]
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: $13/27 \approx 0.4815$; the conditional law is uniform order statistics, $S_{(2)}/T\sim\mathrm{Beta}(2,2)$.
\item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.001$ on the decimal.
\item Acceptable equivalent forms: $13/27$; the integral $\int_{1/3}^{2/3}6x(1-x)\,dx$.
\item Deterministic verifier: closed-form integral plus Monte-Carlo of $\operatorname{median}$ of three uniforms giving $\approx 0.482$ (see \path{_verify.py}).
\end{itemize}
Mark incorrect: answer $1/3$ (treated $S_{(2)}$ as a single uniform, ignoring that it is the median); using exponential-ISI reasoning conditioned on the count; or a Beta$(2,2)$ density with the wrong normalizing constant.
\end{gradingscheme}

\begin{editornotes}
Two layers: (i) the order-statistics characterization of a Poisson process conditioned on its count, and (ii) recognizing the middle of three points is Beta$(2,2)$, not uniform. A model that reports $1/3$ has implicitly used the marginal of a single uniform and missed the median structure entirely.
\end{editornotes}

\end{msbproblem}

\end{document}

response
## Approach This problem tests two ideas: (i) the classical conditioning property of a Poisson process, and (ii) the Beta distribution of a middle order statistic. --- ## Part 1: Conditional Joint Distribution **Key theorem:** Given that a homogeneous Poisson process of rate λ produces exactly n events in [0,T], the conditional joint distribution of the (unordered) event times is that of n i.i.d. Uniform[0,T] random variables. This is independent of λ. **Proof sketch:** The Poisson probability of n events in [0,T] is e^{-λT}(λT)^n/n!. For any 0 < t1 < t2 <... < tn < T, the probability of events in infinitesimal intervals [ti, ti+dt] and no events elsewhere is (λdt)^n · e^{-λT}. Dividing by the marginal P(N=n) gives the joint density n!/T^n for the ordered times, which equals 3!/T^3 = 6/T^3 here. **Answer:** The conditional joint density of (S_(1), S_(2), S_(3)) given N(0,T)=3 is: f(s1, s2, s3) = 6/T³, for 0 < s1 < s2 < s3 < T, and 0 elsewhere. Equivalently, the ordered spike times are the order statistics of 3 i.i.d. Uniform[0,T] variables. --- ## Part 2: P(S_(2) ∈ [T/3, 2T/3]) **Rescale:** Set T=1 without loss of generality (the answer is dimensionless and independent of T). Let U_(2) be the second order statistic of 3 i.i.d. Uniform[0,1] variables. **Distribution of U_(2):** The k-th order statistic from n i.i.d. Uniform[0,1] variables has density: f_{k:n}(x) = n!/[(k-1)!(n-k)!] · x^{k-1}(1-x)^{n-k} For k=2, n=3: f_{2:3}(x) = 3!/(1! · 1!) · x^1(1-x)^1 = 6x(1-x), x ∈ [0,1] This is the Beta(2,2) distribution. **Integration:** P(1/3 ≤ U_(2) ≤ 2/3) = ∫_{1/3}^{2/3} 6x(1-x) dx Antiderivative: ∫(6x - 6x²) dx = 3x² - 2x³ At x = 2/3: 3(4/9) - 2(8/27) = 12/9 - 16/27 = 36/27 - 16/27 = 20/27 At x = 1/3: 3(1/9) - 2(1/27) = 3/9 - 2/27 = 9/27 - 2/27 = 7/27 Therefore: P(S_(2) ∈ [T/3, 2T/3]) = 20/27 - 7/27 = **13/27 ≈ 0.4815** **Verification:** Monte Carlo simulation with 10^6 trials (median of 3 uniforms in [1/3, 2/3]) gives ≈ 0.4813, consistent with 13/27 = 0.48148... **Common error to avoid:** Treating S_(2) as a single Uniform[0,T] variable would give P(S_(2) ∈ [T/3, 2T/3]) = 1/3 ≈ 0.333, which is wrong. The middle spike is not uniform — it has the Beta(2,2) density which is bell-shaped, concentrated near T/2, making it more likely to fall in the central third than a uniform point would be. --- ## Final Answers **(1)** (S_(1), S_(2), S_(3)) | N=3 are the order statistics of 3 i.i.d. Uniform[0,T]; joint density = 6/T³ on the simplex 0 < s1 < s2 < s3 < T. **(2)** P(S_(2) ∈ [T/3, 2T/3]) = **13/27 ≈ 0.4815**

**13/27** Work: Conditional on exactly 3 spikes, the unordered arrival times are i.i.d. Uniform(0,T), independent of λ and T. The middle time S2 is the 2nd order statistic of 3 i.i.d. uniforms; U = S2/T has density f(u) = 6u(1−u) on [0,1]. P(S2 ∈ [T/3, 2T/3]) = ∫_{1/3}^{2/3} 6u(1−u) du = [3u² − 2u³]_{1/3}^{2/3} = 20/27 − 7/27 = 13/27 ≈ 0.4815. Monte Carlo (4×10^6 samples) gives 0.4812, confirming. Exact answer: 13/27.

setupanswertok in/outtime·costtrace
free-subagent k0(1) Order statistics of 3 i.i.d. Uniform[0,T] points. (2) 13/27 ≈ 0.4815. ✗ fail0/0—

response
Problem: Homogeneous Poisson process rate λ on [0,T]. Condition on exactly three spikes, ordered times 0 < S_(1) < S_(2) < S_(3) < T. Part (1) — Conditional joint distribution. A fundamental property of the homogeneous Poisson process: given that exactly n events occur in [0,T], the unordered event times are distributed as n i.i.d. Uniform[0,T] random variables. Equivalently, the ordered times (S_(1),...,S_(n)) are the order statistics of n i.i.d. Uniform[0,T] variables, with conditional joint density f(s_1,...,s_n) = n!/T^n on 0 < s_1 <... < s_n < T. For n=3: f(s_1,s_2,s_3) = 6/T^3 on 0 < s_1 < s_2 < s_3 < T. This is independent of λ. Part (2) — Middle spike in the central third. Rescale to T=1 (scale-invariant). Let U_(2) be the median of three i.i.d. Uniform[0,1] variables. The k-th order statistic of n uniforms is Beta(k, n-k+1); here k=2, n=3, so U_(2) ~ Beta(2,2) with density f(x) = [Γ(4)/(Γ(2)Γ(2))] x^(2-1)(1-x)^(2-1) = 6 x(1-x), 0≤x≤1. Compute: P(1/3 ≤ U_(2) ≤ 2/3) = ∫_{1/3}^{2/3} 6x(1-x) dx = [3x^2 - 2x^3]_{1/3}^{2/3}. At x=2/3: 3·(4/9) − 2·(8/27) = 12/9 − 16/27 = 36/27 − 16/27 = 20/27. At x=1/3: 3·(1/9) − 2·(1/27) = 3/9 − 2/27 = 9/27 − 2/27 = 7/27. Difference: 20/27 − 7/27 = 13/27 ≈ 0.4815. Note: treating S_(2) as a single uniform would wrongly give 1/3; the median structure (Beta(2,2)) gives 13/27. Final answers: (1) Order statistics of 3 i.i.d. Uniform[0,T]; conditional density 6/T^3 on 0<s_1<s_2<s_3<T; independent of λ. (2) 13/27 ≈ 0.4815.
🗒️ Contributor’s own notes / pasted responses

Your own review sidecar — the responses you collected and your own failure-mode read. Shown verbatim, as your words (distinct from the bot review and the model sweep below).

Your failure-mode summary:

  • Observed failure mode: ignoring that the middle spike is the median (Beta(2,2)), treating it as a single uniform
  • Summary: Hypothesized pattern: the headline error is reporting `1/3` by treating the middle spike as a single Uniform[0,T] variable and computing the probability of landing in the central third, thereby ignoring that S_(2) is the median of three points (Beta(2,2)). A subtler error is invoking exponential-ISI reasoning despite conditioning on the count, which destroys the uniform-order-statistics structure. Models that recall the order-statistics fact but use the wrong Beta parameters (e.g., Beta(2,1)) are near-misses.

"""Deterministic verifier — contributor_02 / problem_08.
Conditional Spike Timing: Median in the Central Third. Hand-authored (Claude Code, autonomous; via @yulinl2).
"""

import re
from fractions import Fraction

_MARKERS = [
 r"final\s+answer\s*[:=]?", r"the\s+(?:exact\s+)?answer\s+is\s*[:=]?",
 r"answer\s*[:=]", r"the\s+rate\s+is\s*[:=]?", r"therefore[,:]?",
 r"thus[,:]?", r"hence[,:]?",
]
_MARKER_RE = re.compile("|".join(_MARKERS), re.IGNORECASE)

def _boxed_inner(s):
 """Brace-balanced contents of every \\boxed{...} (handles nested \\frac)."""
 out, i = [], 0
 while True:
 j = s.find("boxed", i)
 if j < 0:
 break
 k = s.find("{", j)
 if k < 0:
 break
 depth, m = 0, k
 while m < len(s):
 if s[m] == "{":
 depth += 1
 elif s[m] == "}":
 depth -= 1
 if depth == 0:
 break
 m += 1
 out.append(s[k + 1:m])
 i = m + 1
 return out

def _normalize(s):
 """LaTeX -> plain so rationals survive: \\frac{a}{b} -> a/b, keep %.
 (c09 fractions are always integer/integer, so the flat a/b form is exact;
 a compound numerator would need parens, not present here.)"""
 # capture an optional leading minus OUTSIDE the frac (-\frac{a}{b} -> -a/b):
 # a detached "-" would otherwise be split off and the component read positive.
 s = re.sub(r"(-?)\s*\\[dt]?frac\s*\{([^{}]*)\}\s*\{([^{}]*)\}", r" \1\2/\3 ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\(left|right|!|,|;|:|quad|qquad)", " ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\text\s*\{[^{}]*\}", " ", s)
 s = s.replace(r"\%", "%").replace(r"\$", " ").replace("$", " ")
 s = s.replace("{,}", "") # 225{,}000 -> 225000
 s = s.replace("\\", " ").replace("{", " ").replace("}", " ")
 # thousands separator ONLY when strictly adjacent (1,250 -> 1250); never join
 # across a space (a vector's "1907, 379" must stay two numbers).
 s = re.sub(r"(\d),(\d{3})(?!\d)", r"\1\2", s)
 return s

# token: percent | fraction a/b | decimal | integer (left-to-right, non-overlap)
_TOK = re.compile(
 r"(?P<pct>-?\d+(?:\.\d+)?)\s*%"
 r"|(?P<n>-?\d+)\s*/\s*(?P<d>-?\d+)"
 r"|(?P<dec>-?\d+\.\d+)"
 r"|(?P<int>-?\d+)")

def _rationals(zone):
 """Ordered list of Fraction values committed in `zone` (already a string)."""
 out = []
 for m in _TOK.finditer(_normalize(zone)):
 try:
 if m.group("pct") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("pct")) / 100)
 elif m.group("n") is not None:
 d = int(m.group("d"))
 if d == 0:
 continue
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("n")), d))
 elif m.group("dec") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("dec")))
 else:
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("int"))))
 except (ValueError, ZeroDivisionError):
 continue
 return out

def _final_zone(response):
 """The text of the model's committed final answer (most -> least reliable):
 boxed contents; else the last marker line's tail; else the last line."""
 if not response or not response.strip:
 return ""
 boxed = _boxed_inner(response)
 if boxed:
 return " ".join(boxed)
 lines = [ln for ln in response.splitlines if ln.strip]
 # last marker line that actually carries a number (weak discourse markers
 # like "therefore/thus/hence" otherwise hijack the zone to a number-less
 # trailing comment, burying the real commit on the line above).
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 m = _MARKER_RE.search(ln)
 if m and any(c.isdigit for c in ln[m.start:]):
 return ln[m.start:]
 # else the last line that carries a number
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 if any(c.isdigit for c in ln):
 return ln
 return lines[-1] if lines else ""

def _committed_rational(zone):
 """The single COMMITTED final rational in `zone`: the value after the LAST
 '=' if one is present (so an arithmetic line like '105 - 105 = 0' commits 0,
 NOT the intermediate 105 — the loose 'oracle appears anywhere' false-positive
 the cold-read found), else the last rational. Returns (value|None, all)."""
 cands = _rationals(zone)
 if not cands:
 return None, []
 norm = _normalize(zone)
 if "=" in norm:
 tail = _rationals(norm.rsplit("=", 1)[1])
 if tail:
 return tail[-1], cands
 # no '=': the committed value is the FIRST rational (right after the marker /
 # at the head of the line) — a trailing parenthetical like "0 (note 105)" or
 # "253 (> 200)" must not steal the verdict.
 return cands[0], cands

def match_exact(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction. The COMMITTED final must equal it EXACTLY (no tolerance
 — rounded-only decimals are insufficient per the gradingscheme). Grades the
 committed value, not any number in the zone (no substring/intermediate pass)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 if committed == oracle:
 return True, "committed %s == oracle %s" % (committed, oracle)
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (cands %s)" % (
 committed, oracle, [str(c) for c in cands])

def match_vector(response, oracle):
 """oracle: list[Fraction]. Every component must appear in the final zone."""
 cands = set(_rationals(_final_zone(response)))
 missing = [str(o) for o in oracle if o not in cands]
 if not missing:
 return True, "all %d components present" % len(oracle)
 return False, "missing/incorrect components: %s" % missing

def match_rate(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction rate (e.g. 39/125). Accept the exact fraction, the
 decimal (tol 1e-4, the percent form is inherently rounded), or the percent
 (bare integer like 31.2 read as 31.2%)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 r = oracle
 rf = float(r)
 cf = float(committed)
 if committed == r:
 return True, "exact %s == oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf) < 1e-4:
 return True, "decimal %s ~= oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf * 100) < 1e-2:
 return True, "percent %s ~= oracle %s%%" % (committed, float(r * 100))
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (=%.6g; cands %s)" % (
 committed, r, rf, [str(c) for c in cands])


def grade(response: str, context: dict) -> dict:
 """Conditional Spike Timing: Median in the Central Third

 Oracle: Fraction(13,27) (author answerkey, sympy-reconfirmed). Accept the EXACT
 reduced fraction OR a 4-decimal value (tol 5e-5); a coarsely-rounded
 decimal alone is insufficient (gradingscheme asks for both forms)."""
 oracle = Fraction(13,27)
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return {"pass": False, "score": 0.0, "notes": "no rational final answer extracted"}
 if committed == oracle or abs(float(committed) - float(oracle)) < 5e-5:
 return {"pass": True, "score": 1.0,
 "notes": "committed %s matches oracle %s" % (committed, oracle)}
 return {"pass": False, "score": 0.0,
 "notes": "committed %s != oracle %s (cands %s)" % (committed, oracle, [str(c) for c in cands])}

 out = grade(response=data['response'], context=data['context'])
 print(json.dumps(out))

#09

Discounted Value Function on a Reward Cycle

📄 Submission snapshot — parsed source evidence (private bank, unreleased; do not redistribute)
Consider a deterministic three-state Markov reward process on states $\{A, B, C\}$ that cycles $A \to B \to C \to A \to \cdots$. Rewards are received \emph{on transition}strong: Good — explicitly disambiguates the convention that trips most solvers.: the transition $B\to C$ yields reward $+1$, and all other transitions yield $0$. The discount factor is $\gamma = 0.9$. Define the value $V(s) = \mathbb{E}\big[\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}\gamma^k R_{k+1} \mid S_0 = s\big]$, where $R_{k+1}$ is the reward received on the $k$-th transition out of the current statepraise: Pinning down the index convention here is exactly what makes the problem unambiguous.. Give $V(A)$, $V(B)$, and $V(C)$ as exact reduced fractions.

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

The Bellman equations (reward received on the transition, then discount the next state's value) are \[ V(A) = 0 + \gamma V(B),\quad V(B) = 1 + \gamma V(C),\quad V(C) = 0 + \gamma V(A). \] Substitute around the cycle: \[ V(C) = \gamma V(A) = \gamma\,\gamma V(B) = \gamma^2 V(B) = \gamma^2\big(1+\gamma V(C)\big) \;\Longrightarrow\; V(C)\,(1-\gamma^3) = \gamma^2, \] so $V(C) = \dfrac{\gamma^2}{1-\gamma^3}$, and then $V(B)=\dfrac{1}{1-\gamma^3}$, $V(A)=\dfrac{\gamma}{1-\gamma^3}$. With $\gamma = 9/10$: $\gamma^2 = 81/100$, $\gamma^3 = 729/1000$, $1-\gamma^3 = 271/1000$. Hence \[ V(A) = \frac{(9/10)}{271/1000} = \boxed{\dfrac{900}{271}}\approx 3.3210,\quad V(B) = \boxed{\dfrac{1000}{271}}\approx 3.6900,\quad V(C) = \boxed{\dfrac{810}{271}}\approx 2.9889. \]

Rubric (grading scheme) — how a verifier is meant to grade an answer.

\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: $V(A)=900/271$, $V(B)=1000/271$, $V(C)=810/271$. \item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.001$ on each decimal. \item Acceptable equivalent forms: the closed forms $\gamma/(1-\gamma^3)$, $1/(1-\gamma^3)$, $\gamma^2/(1-\gamma^3)$. \item Deterministic verifier: solve the $3\times3$ linear Bellman system and compare (see \path{_verify.py}). \end{itemize} Mark incorrect: any solution where $V(B)$ is not the largest (the reward is collected leaving $B$); using $1/(1-\gamma)$ normalization (treating reward every step); or a one-step discount offset (e.g.\ $V(B)=\gamma/(1-\gamma^3)$).

\begin{document}

% Keep this file in sync with the matching problem_09_review.md sidecar.

\problemsetup
 {Discounted Value Function on a Reward Cycle}
 {Statistics / Mathematics}
 {Reinforcement learning / Markov reward processes}
 {Qualifying-exam-plus}
 {Contributor 02 (Dongyu Gong)}
 {\href{mailto:dongyu.gong@yale.edu}{dongyu.gong@yale.edu}}
 {Ready for review}
\problemtags
 {compute / derive}
 {Bellman fixed-point algebra, reward-timing and discount conventions}
 {exact match (rational)}
 {off-by-one in when the reward is discounted, or geometric-series errors}
\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
Consider a deterministic three-state Markov reward process on states $\{A, B, C\}$ that cycles $A \to B \to C \to A \to \cdots$. Rewards are received \emph{on transition}: the transition $B\to C$ yields reward $+1$, and all other transitions yield $0$. The discount factor is $\gamma = 0.9$.

Define the value $V(s) = \mathbb{E}\big[\sum_{k=0}^{\infty}\gamma^k R_{k+1} \mid S_0 = s\big]$, where $R_{k+1}$ is the reward received on the $k$-th transition out of the current state.

Give $V(A)$, $V(B)$, and $V(C)$ as exact reduced fractions.
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
The Bellman equations (reward received on the transition, then discount the next state's value) are
\[
V(A) = 0 + \gamma V(B),\quad
V(B) = 1 + \gamma V(C),\quad
V(C) = 0 + \gamma V(A).
\]
Substitute around the cycle:
\[
V(C) = \gamma V(A) = \gamma\,\gamma V(B) = \gamma^2 V(B) = \gamma^2\big(1+\gamma V(C)\big)
\;\Longrightarrow\; V(C)\,(1-\gamma^3) = \gamma^2,
\]
so $V(C) = \dfrac{\gamma^2}{1-\gamma^3}$, and then $V(B)=\dfrac{1}{1-\gamma^3}$, $V(A)=\dfrac{\gamma}{1-\gamma^3}$.

With $\gamma = 9/10$: $\gamma^2 = 81/100$, $\gamma^3 = 729/1000$, $1-\gamma^3 = 271/1000$. Hence
\[
V(A) = \frac{(9/10)}{271/1000} = \boxed{\dfrac{900}{271}}\approx 3.3210,\quad
V(B) = \boxed{\dfrac{1000}{271}}\approx 3.6900,\quad
V(C) = \boxed{\dfrac{810}{271}}\approx 2.9889.
\]
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: exact numerical answer.}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: $V(A)=900/271$, $V(B)=1000/271$, $V(C)=810/271$.
\item Numerical tolerance: $\pm 0.001$ on each decimal.
\item Acceptable equivalent forms: the closed forms $\gamma/(1-\gamma^3)$, $1/(1-\gamma^3)$, $\gamma^2/(1-\gamma^3)$.
\item Deterministic verifier: solve the $3\times3$ linear Bellman system and compare (see \path{_verify.py}).
\end{itemize}
Mark incorrect: any solution where $V(B)$ is not the largest (the reward is collected leaving $B$); using $1/(1-\gamma)$ normalization (treating reward every step); or a one-step discount offset (e.g.\ $V(B)=\gamma/(1-\gamma^3)$).
\end{gradingscheme}

\begin{editornotes}
The discount-timing convention is the trap: with reward on the $B\to C$ transition, $B$ has the highest value, not $C$. Small off-by-one errors in where $\gamma$ is applied produce a self-consistent but wrong set of values, so the rubric checks the ordering and the exact denominators $1-\gamma^3$.
\end{editornotes}

\end{msbproblem}

\end{document}

## Problem: Discounted Value Function on a Reward Cycle **Setup:** Deterministic MRP on states {A, B, C} cycling A → B → C → A →... Reward +1 on the B→C transition, 0 on all others. Discount γ = 0.9. Value function: V(s) = E[Σ_{k=0}^∞ γ^k R_{k+1} | S_0 = s] where R_{k+1} is the reward on the k-th transition out of the current state. --- ### Step 1: Write Bellman Equations The Bellman equation for a deterministic process is V(s) = R(s → s') + γ · V(s'), where R(s → s') is the immediate reward earned when leaving state s. - **State A** (transition A→B, reward 0): V(A) = 0 + γ · V(B) - **State B** (transition B→C, reward 1): V(B) = 1 + γ · V(C) - **State C** (transition C→A, reward 0): V(C) = 0 + γ · V(A) --- ### Step 2: Solve the System From the first and third equations: V(C) = γ · V(A) = γ · (γ · V(B)) = γ² · V(B) Substitute into the second equation: V(B) = 1 + γ · V(C) = 1 + γ · γ² · V(B) = 1 + γ³ · V(B) V(B)(1 − γ³) = 1 V(B) = 1 / (1 − γ³) Then: V(A) = γ · V(B) = γ / (1 − γ³) V(C) = γ² · V(B) = γ² / (1 − γ³) --- ### Step 3: Substitute γ = 9/10 γ² = 81/100 γ³ = 729/1000 1 − γ³ = (1000 − 729)/1000 = 271/1000 V(B) = 1 / (271/1000) = 1000/271 V(A) = (9/10) / (271/1000) = (9/10) × (1000/271) = 9000/2710 = 900/271 V(C) = (81/100) / (271/1000) = (81/100) × (1000/271) = 81000/27100 = 810/271 --- ### Step 4: Verify Fractions Are Fully Reduced 271 is prime (checked: not divisible by 2, 3, 5, 7, 11, 13, and 17² = 289 > 271). - gcd(900, 271) = 1 (since 271 is prime and does not divide 900) - gcd(1000, 271) = 1 (since 271 is prime and does not divide 1000) - gcd(810, 271) = 1 (since 271 is prime and does not divide 810) All three fractions are already fully reduced. --- ### Final Answer V(A) = 900/271 ≈ 3.321 V(B) = 1000/271 ≈ 3.690 V(C) = 810/271 ≈ 2.989 Note: V(B) is the largest because the reward (+1) is collected immediately when leaving state B. V(A) is next (one step before the reward), and V(C) is the smallest (two steps away from the next reward).

Problem: Deterministic 3-state Markov reward process cycling A→B→C→A→… Rewards on transition: B→C yields +1, all others 0. γ=0.9. Value V(s)=E[Σ_{k≥0} γ^k R_{k+1} | S_0=s]. Find V(A), V(B), V(C) as exact reduced fractions. Approach — Bellman fixed point with reward-on-transition timing. The reward earned on leaving a state is undiscounted at that state; the successor's value is discounted by one factor of γ: V(A) = 0 + γ V(B) V(B) = 1 + γ V(C) V(C) = 0 + γ V(A) Solve by substituting around the cycle. From the first and third equations: V(A) = γ V(B), V(C) = γ V(A) = γ² V(B). Plug into the middle equation: V(B) = 1 + γ V(C) = 1 + γ·(γ² V(B)) = 1 + γ³ V(B). Hence V(B)(1 − γ³) = 1, so V(B) = 1/(1 − γ³), V(A) = γ/(1 − γ³), V(C) = γ²/(1 − γ³). Sanity check on ordering: since 0<γ<1, V(B) > V(A) > V(C), i.e. the state from which the +1 reward is collected (leaving B) has the largest value. This rules out the off-by-one trap that would make C largest. Numerics with γ = 9/10: γ² = 81/100, γ³ = 729/1000, 1 − γ³ = 271/1000. V(A) = (9/10)/(271/1000) = 9000/2710 = 900/271 ≈ 3.3210 V(B) = 1/(271/1000) = 1000/271 ≈ 3.6900 V(C) = (81/100)/(271/1000) = 81000/27100 = 810/271 ≈ 2.9889 271 is prime and divides none of 900, 1000, 810, so all fractions are already in lowest terms. Final answer: V(A) = 900/271 V(B) = 1000/271 V(C) = 810/271
🗒️ Contributor’s own notes / pasted responses

Your own review sidecar — the responses you collected and your own failure-mode read. Shown verbatim, as your words (distinct from the bot review and the model sweep below).

Your failure-mode summary:

  • Observed failure mode: off-by-one in when the reward is discounted, or geometric-series errors
  • Summary: Hypothesized pattern: (i) a one-step discount offset (applying gamma to the reward as if received on entering the next state), which permutes which state has the highest value; (ii) using a `1/(1-gamma)` normalization as if reward arrives every step; (iii) arithmetic slips in evaluating `1-gamma^3 = 271/1000`. The reward-timing convention (reward on the B->C transition) makes B the highest-value state, and answers that put C highest have mis-timed the discount.

"""Deterministic verifier — contributor_02 / problem_09.
Discounted Value Function on a Reward Cycle. Hand-authored (Claude Code, autonomous; via @yulinl2).
"""

import re
from fractions import Fraction

_MARKERS = [
 r"final\s+answer\s*[:=]?", r"the\s+(?:exact\s+)?answer\s+is\s*[:=]?",
 r"answer\s*[:=]", r"the\s+rate\s+is\s*[:=]?", r"therefore[,:]?",
 r"thus[,:]?", r"hence[,:]?",
]
_MARKER_RE = re.compile("|".join(_MARKERS), re.IGNORECASE)

def _boxed_inner(s):
 """Brace-balanced contents of every \\boxed{...} (handles nested \\frac)."""
 out, i = [], 0
 while True:
 j = s.find("boxed", i)
 if j < 0:
 break
 k = s.find("{", j)
 if k < 0:
 break
 depth, m = 0, k
 while m < len(s):
 if s[m] == "{":
 depth += 1
 elif s[m] == "}":
 depth -= 1
 if depth == 0:
 break
 m += 1
 out.append(s[k + 1:m])
 i = m + 1
 return out

def _normalize(s):
 """LaTeX -> plain so rationals survive: \\frac{a}{b} -> a/b, keep %.
 (c09 fractions are always integer/integer, so the flat a/b form is exact;
 a compound numerator would need parens, not present here.)"""
 # capture an optional leading minus OUTSIDE the frac (-\frac{a}{b} -> -a/b):
 # a detached "-" would otherwise be split off and the component read positive.
 s = re.sub(r"(-?)\s*\\[dt]?frac\s*\{([^{}]*)\}\s*\{([^{}]*)\}", r" \1\2/\3 ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\(left|right|!|,|;|:|quad|qquad)", " ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\text\s*\{[^{}]*\}", " ", s)
 s = s.replace(r"\%", "%").replace(r"\$", " ").replace("$", " ")
 s = s.replace("{,}", "") # 225{,}000 -> 225000
 s = s.replace("\\", " ").replace("{", " ").replace("}", " ")
 # thousands separator ONLY when strictly adjacent (1,250 -> 1250); never join
 # across a space (a vector's "1907, 379" must stay two numbers).
 s = re.sub(r"(\d),(\d{3})(?!\d)", r"\1\2", s)
 return s

# token: percent | fraction a/b | decimal | integer (left-to-right, non-overlap)
_TOK = re.compile(
 r"(?P<pct>-?\d+(?:\.\d+)?)\s*%"
 r"|(?P<n>-?\d+)\s*/\s*(?P<d>-?\d+)"
 r"|(?P<dec>-?\d+\.\d+)"
 r"|(?P<int>-?\d+)")

def _rationals(zone):
 """Ordered list of Fraction values committed in `zone` (already a string)."""
 out = []
 for m in _TOK.finditer(_normalize(zone)):
 try:
 if m.group("pct") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("pct")) / 100)
 elif m.group("n") is not None:
 d = int(m.group("d"))
 if d == 0:
 continue
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("n")), d))
 elif m.group("dec") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("dec")))
 else:
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("int"))))
 except (ValueError, ZeroDivisionError):
 continue
 return out

def _final_zone(response):
 """The text of the model's committed final answer (most -> least reliable):
 boxed contents; else the last marker line's tail; else the last line."""
 if not response or not response.strip:
 return ""
 boxed = _boxed_inner(response)
 if boxed:
 return " ".join(boxed)
 lines = [ln for ln in response.splitlines if ln.strip]
 # last marker line that actually carries a number (weak discourse markers
 # like "therefore/thus/hence" otherwise hijack the zone to a number-less
 # trailing comment, burying the real commit on the line above).
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 m = _MARKER_RE.search(ln)
 if m and any(c.isdigit for c in ln[m.start:]):
 return ln[m.start:]
 # else the last line that carries a number
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 if any(c.isdigit for c in ln):
 return ln
 return lines[-1] if lines else ""

def _committed_rational(zone):
 """The single COMMITTED final rational in `zone`: the value after the LAST
 '=' if one is present (so an arithmetic line like '105 - 105 = 0' commits 0,
 NOT the intermediate 105 — the loose 'oracle appears anywhere' false-positive
 the cold-read found), else the last rational. Returns (value|None, all)."""
 cands = _rationals(zone)
 if not cands:
 return None, []
 norm = _normalize(zone)
 if "=" in norm:
 tail = _rationals(norm.rsplit("=", 1)[1])
 if tail:
 return tail[-1], cands
 # no '=': the committed value is the FIRST rational (right after the marker /
 # at the head of the line) — a trailing parenthetical like "0 (note 105)" or
 # "253 (> 200)" must not steal the verdict.
 return cands[0], cands

def match_exact(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction. The COMMITTED final must equal it EXACTLY (no tolerance
 — rounded-only decimals are insufficient per the gradingscheme). Grades the
 committed value, not any number in the zone (no substring/intermediate pass)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 if committed == oracle:
 return True, "committed %s == oracle %s" % (committed, oracle)
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (cands %s)" % (
 committed, oracle, [str(c) for c in cands])

def match_vector(response, oracle):
 """oracle: list[Fraction]. Every component must appear in the final zone."""
 cands = set(_rationals(_final_zone(response)))
 missing = [str(o) for o in oracle if o not in cands]
 if not missing:
 return True, "all %d components present" % len(oracle)
 return False, "missing/incorrect components: %s" % missing

def match_rate(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction rate (e.g. 39/125). Accept the exact fraction, the
 decimal (tol 1e-4, the percent form is inherently rounded), or the percent
 (bare integer like 31.2 read as 31.2%)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 r = oracle
 rf = float(r)
 cf = float(committed)
 if committed == r:
 return True, "exact %s == oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf) < 1e-4:
 return True, "decimal %s ~= oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf * 100) < 1e-2:
 return True, "percent %s ~= oracle %s%%" % (committed, float(r * 100))
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (=%.6g; cands %s)" % (
 committed, r, rf, [str(c) for c in cands])


import re as _re
from fractions import Fraction as _Fr
def grade(response: str, context: dict) -> dict:
 """Discounted Value Function on a Reward Cycle

 Three LABELED values: V(A)=900/271, V(B)=1000/271, V(C)=810/271 — POSITIONAL,
 so a set/positional match would pass an A/B SWAP or a hedged body that merely
 contains the right triple (cold-read 2026-06-18, FA-3/FA-5). So grade STRICTLY
 by LABEL: every V(A)/V(B)/V(C) must carry its exact-fraction target. The
 response is normalized first so \\frac{900}{271} == 900/271 (cold-read FR-6),
 and labels accept V(A)/V[A]/V_A/V(a). No positional fallback (its false-accepts
 outweigh the convenience; an unlabeled bare triple is an incomplete answer to
 "Give V(A), V(B), V(C)"). The problem demands EXACT reduced fractions, so a
 decimal value correctly fails."""
 targets = [("A", _Fr(900, 271)), ("B", _Fr(1000, 271)), ("C", _Fr(810, 271))]
 norm = _normalize(response) # \frac{a}{b} -> a/b; keeps the V(X) text
 labeled = {}
 for m in _re.finditer(r"V\s*[\(\[_{]?\s*([ABCabc])\s*[\)\]}]?\s*[=:]\s*"
 r"(-?\d+\s*/\s*\d+|-?\d+\.\d+|-?\d+)", norm):
 tok = m.group(2).replace(" ", "")
 try:
 f = _Fr(tok) if "/" not in tok else _Fr(int(tok.split("/")[0]), int(tok.split("/")[1]))
 except Exception:
 continue
 labeled.setdefault(m.group(1).upper, f) # first labeled value wins
 if not {"A", "B", "C"}.issubset(labeled):
 return {"pass": False, "score": 0.0,
 "notes": "need labeled V(A),V(B),V(C); got %s" % sorted(labeled)}
 ok = all(labeled.get(k) == v for k, v in targets)
 return {"pass": bool(ok), "score": 1.0 if ok else 0.0,
 "notes": "labeled %s" % {k: str(labeled[k]) for k in "ABC"}}


if __name__ == '__main__':
 import sys, json
 data = json.load(sys.stdin)
 out = grade(response=data['response'], context=data['context'])
 print(json.dumps(out))

Laplacian Spectrum of a Ring Network

Consider an undirected ring network of $N=6$ neuronssuggestion: For a benchmark targeting frontier models, $N=6$ makes the spectrum guessable by pattern-matching alone. Consider a larger or asymmetric variant (e.g. weighted ring, or ring with one chord) that preserves circulant structure but stumps recall-based solving., where neuron $i$ is connected only to its two ring neighbors $i\pm 1 \pmod 6$, each edge with weight $1$. Let $L = D - A$ be the (unnormalized) graph Laplacian, where $A$ is the adjacency matrix and $D = \operatorname{diag}(\deg)$. \begin{enumerate} \item Give the complete multiset of eigenvalues of $L$.ambiguity: Minor: specify the expected output format (sorted list with multiplicities vs. closed-form $\mu_k$ expression). The grading scheme allows both, but the statement could say so. \item Give the algebraic connectivity (smallest nonzero eigenvalue) and the number of \emph{distinct} eigenvalues. \end{enumerate}

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

$L$ is a symmetric circulant matrix (every node has degree $2$), with first row $(2,-1,0,0,0,-1)$. Its eigenvalues are \[ \mu_k = 2 - 2\cos\!\Big(\frac{2\pi k}{N}\Big), \qquad k = 0,1,\dots,N-1, \] (the cycle $C_N$ Laplacian spectrum). For $N=6$: \[ \begin{aligned} k=0:\ & 2-2\cos 0 = 0,\\ k=1,5:\ & 2-2\cos(60^\circ) = 2-1 = 1,\\ k=2,4:\ & 2-2\cos(120^\circ) = 2+1 = 3,\\ k=3:\ & 2-2\cos(180^\circ) = 2+2 = 4. \end{aligned} \] \textbf{(1)} Eigenvalue multiset: $\{0,\,1,\,1,\,3,\,3,\,4\}$. \textbf{(2)} Algebraic connectivity $= 1$ (smallest nonzero eigenvalue, multiplicity $2$); number of distinct eigenvalues $= 4$, namely $\{0,1,3,4\}$. (Trace check: $0+1+1+3+3+4 = 12 = 2N = \sum_i \deg_i$.)

Rubric (grading scheme) — how a verifier is meant to grade an answer.

\textbf{Mode A: exact eigenvalue multiset.} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: $\{0,1,1,3,3,4\}$; algebraic connectivity $=1$; $4$ distinct eigenvalues. \item Numerical tolerance: none (integers). \item Acceptable equivalent forms: eigenvalues listed with multiplicities; $\mu_k = 2-2\cos(2\pi k/6)$. \item Deterministic verifier: compare the sorted multiset to $[0,1,1,3,3,4]$ and against \texttt{numpy.linalg.eigvalsh} of the explicit $6\times6$ Laplacian (see \path{_verify.py}). \end{itemize} Mark incorrect: spectra based on $2-2\cos(\pi k/6)$ (path graph $P_6$, which would give $0,\,2-\sqrt3,\,1,\,2,\,3,\,2+\sqrt3$); any spectrum whose trace $\ne 12$; or a negative eigenvalue (sign error in $D-A$).

\begin{document}

% Keep this file in sync with the matching problem_10_review.md sidecar.

\problemsetup
 {Laplacian Spectrum of a Ring Network}
 {Mathematics}
 {Spectral graph theory / circulant matrices}
 {Qualifying-exam-plus}
 {Contributor 02 (Dongyu Gong)}
 {\href{mailto:dongyu.gong@yale.edu}{dongyu.gong@yale.edu}}
 {Ready for review}
\problemtags
 {compute / derive}
 {circulant diagonalization, distinguishing ring (cycle) from path boundary conditions}
 {exact match (multiset of eigenvalues)}
 {using path-graph $\cos(\pi k/N)$ instead of ring $\cos(2\pi k/N)$, or a sign error in the Laplacian}
\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
Consider an undirected ring network of $N=6$ neurons, where neuron $i$ is connected only to its two ring neighbors $i\pm 1 \pmod 6$, each edge with weight $1$. Let $L = D - A$ be the (unnormalized) graph Laplacian, where $A$ is the adjacency matrix and $D = \operatorname{diag}(\deg)$.
\begin{enumerate}
\item Give the complete multiset of eigenvalues of $L$.
\item Give the algebraic connectivity (smallest nonzero eigenvalue) and the number of \emph{distinct} eigenvalues.
\end{enumerate}
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
$L$ is a symmetric circulant matrix (every node has degree $2$), with first row $(2,-1,0,0,0,-1)$. Its eigenvalues are
\[
\mu_k = 2 - 2\cos\!\Big(\frac{2\pi k}{N}\Big), \qquad k = 0,1,\dots,N-1,
\]
(the cycle $C_N$ Laplacian spectrum). For $N=6$:
\[
\begin{aligned}
k=0:\ & 2-2\cos 0 = 0,\\
k=1,5:\ & 2-2\cos(60^\circ) = 2-1 = 1,\\
k=2,4:\ & 2-2\cos(120^\circ) = 2+1 = 3,\\
k=3:\ & 2-2\cos(180^\circ) = 2+2 = 4.
\end{aligned}
\]
\textbf{(1)} Eigenvalue multiset: $\{0,\,1,\,1,\,3,\,3,\,4\}$.

\textbf{(2)} Algebraic connectivity $= 1$ (smallest nonzero eigenvalue, multiplicity $2$); number of distinct eigenvalues $= 4$, namely $\{0,1,3,4\}$. (Trace check: $0+1+1+3+3+4 = 12 = 2N = \sum_i \deg_i$.)
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: exact eigenvalue multiset.}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: $\{0,1,1,3,3,4\}$; algebraic connectivity $=1$; $4$ distinct eigenvalues.
\item Numerical tolerance: none (integers).
\item Acceptable equivalent forms: eigenvalues listed with multiplicities; $\mu_k = 2-2\cos(2\pi k/6)$.
\item Deterministic verifier: compare the sorted multiset to $[0,1,1,3,3,4]$ and against \texttt{numpy.linalg.eigvalsh} of the explicit $6\times6$ Laplacian (see \path{_verify.py}).
\end{itemize}
Mark incorrect: spectra based on $2-2\cos(\pi k/6)$ (path graph $P_6$, which would give $0,\,2-\sqrt3,\,1,\,2,\,3,\,2+\sqrt3$); any spectrum whose trace $\ne 12$; or a negative eigenvalue (sign error in $D-A$).
\end{gradingscheme}

\begin{editornotes}
The discriminating trap is the boundary condition: a ring (cycle) gives $\cos(2\pi k/N)$ with the symmetric multiplicity pattern, whereas a path/line graph gives $\cos(\pi k/N)$ and an irrational spectrum. The trace identity $\sum\mu_k = 2N$ is a fast falsifier.
\end{editornotes}

\end{msbproblem}

\end{document}

claude-sonnet-4-6 · free-subagent (1 runs — every captured sample for this model × setup; linked from the results matrix above)
setupanswertok in/outtime·costtrace
free-subagent k0(1) Eigenvalue multiset: {0, 1, 1, 3, 3, 4}. (2) Algebraic connectivity = 1; number of distinct eigenvalues = 4. ✗ fail0/0—
trace
response
**Problem:** Laplacian Spectrum of a Ring Network (C_6 cycle graph, N=6, all edge weights = 1). **Approach:** The Laplacian of a cycle graph is a circulant matrix, diagonalizable by the DFT. --- **Step 1: Write down the Laplacian.** Each node has degree 2, so D = 2I_6. The adjacency matrix A has A_{i,j} = 1 when j = i ± 1 (mod 6). L = D - A is the 6×6 circulant matrix with first row (2, -1, 0, 0, 0, -1): L = [[2,-1,0,0,0,-1], [-1,2,-1,0,0,0], [0,-1,2,-1,0,0], [0,0,-1,2,-1,0], [0,0,0,-1,2,-1], [-1,0,0,0,-1,2]] **Step 2: Eigenvalues of a circulant matrix.** For a circulant matrix circ(c_0, c_1,..., c_{N-1}), the eigenvalues are: mu_k = sum_{j=0}^{N-1} c_j * exp(2*pi*i*j*k/N), k = 0, 1,..., N-1. Here c_0 = 2, c_1 = -1, c_5 = -1, c_2 = c_3 = c_4 = 0, N = 6. So: mu_k = 2 - exp(2*pi*i*k/6) - exp(2*pi*i*5k/6) = 2 - exp(2*pi*i*k/6) - exp(-2*pi*i*k/6) [since 5k ≡ -k (mod 6)] = 2 - 2*cos(2*pi*k/6) **Step 3: Evaluate for each k = 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5.** - k = 0: mu_0 = 2 - 2*cos(0) = 2 - 2(1) = 0 - k = 1: mu_1 = 2 - 2*cos(pi/3) = 2 - 2(1/2) = 1 - k = 2: mu_2 = 2 - 2*cos(2*pi/3) = 2 - 2(-1/2) = 3 - k = 3: mu_3 = 2 - 2*cos(pi) = 2 - 2(-1) = 4 - k = 4: mu_4 = 2 - 2*cos(4*pi/3) = 2 - 2(-1/2) = 3 - k = 5: mu_5 = 2 - 2*cos(5*pi/3) = 2 - 2(1/2) = 1 **Trace check:** 0 + 1 + 3 + 4 + 3 + 1 = 12 = 2N = sum of all vertex degrees. Confirmed. --- **Answers:** **(1) Complete multiset of eigenvalues:** {0, 1, 1, 3, 3, 4} The eigenvalues with multiplicities are: 0 (×1), 1 (×2), 3 (×2), 4 (×1). **(2) Algebraic connectivity (Fiedler value):** The smallest nonzero eigenvalue is **1** (with multiplicity 2). **Number of distinct eigenvalues:** **4**, namely {0, 1, 3, 4}. --- **Key trap to avoid:** Confusing the ring (cycle C_N) with a path graph P_N. A path graph has eigenvalues 2 - 2*cos(pi*k/N), k = 0,...,N-1, which yields irrational values for N=6. The ring's periodic boundary conditions give 2 - 2*cos(2*pi*k/N), producing the clean integer spectrum {0,1,1,3,3,4}.

trace
response
Problem: Ring network of N=6 neurons (cycle graph C_6), each node connected to neighbors i±1 mod 6 with unit weight. L = D - A, unnormalized Laplacian. Approach: Every node in C_6 has degree 2, so D = 2I and L is a symmetric circulant matrix with first row (2, -1, 0, 0, 0, -1). Circulant matrices are diagonalized by the discrete Fourier basis, so the eigenvalues follow directly from the generating function evaluated at the N-th roots of unity. For the circulant with first row (c_0, c_1,..., c_{N-1}) = (2, -1, 0, 0, 0, -1), the eigenvalues are μ_k = Σ_j c_j ω^{jk}, ω = e^{2πi/N}, k = 0,...,N-1 = 2 - ω^k - ω^{(N-1)k} = 2 - ω^k - ω^{-k} = 2 - 2cos(2πk/N). This is the standard cycle-graph C_N Laplacian spectrum. (Note: this is the RING/cycle boundary condition giving cos(2πk/N), NOT the path graph P_N which would give cos(πk/N) and an irrational spectrum.) Evaluate for N = 6: k = 0: 2 - 2cos(0) = 2 - 2(1) = 0 k = 1: 2 - 2cos(60°) = 2 - 2(1/2) = 1 k = 2: 2 - 2cos(120°) = 2 - 2(-1/2) = 3 k = 3: 2 - 2cos(180°) = 2 - 2(-1) = 4 k = 4: 2 - 2cos(240°) = 2 - 2(-1/2) = 3 k = 5: 2 - 2cos(300°) = 2 - 2(1/2) = 1 So k=1,5 → 1 (multiplicity 2); k=2,4 → 3 (multiplicity 2); k=0 → 0; k=3 → 4. (1) Complete eigenvalue multiset: {0, 1, 1, 3, 3, 4}. Trace check (falsifier): sum = 0 + 1 + 1 + 3 + 3 + 4 = 12 = 2N = Σ deg_i = 6·2. Consistent. All eigenvalues nonnegative, with a single 0 (connected graph). Good. (2) Algebraic connectivity = smallest nonzero eigenvalue = 1 (multiplicity 2). Number of distinct eigenvalues = 4, namely {0, 1, 3, 4}. Final answer: (1) {0, 1, 1, 3, 3, 4} (2) Algebraic connectivity = 1; number of distinct eigenvalues = 4 ({0, 1, 3, 4}).

  • Summary: Hypothesized pattern: the discriminating error is using the path-graph (line) spectrum `2 - 2 cos(pi k/N)`, which produces an irrational spectrum `{0, 2-sqrt3, 1, 2, 3, 2+sqrt3}` instead of the ring's integer spectrum. Other errors: a sign error in `L = D - A` producing negative eigenvalues, or miscounting multiplicities. #11

Onset of Oscillations in a Linearized E--I Network (Hopf)

📄 Submission snapshot — parsed source evidence (private bank, unreleased; do not redistribute)
A two-population (excitatory $x$, inhibitory $y$) rate network is linearized about a fixed point to \[ \dot{x} = -x + w_{EE}\,x - w_{EI}\,y, \qquad \dot{y} = -y + w_{IE}\,x - w_{II}\,y, \] with $w_{EI} = w_{IE} = \sqrt{5}$praise: Nice parameter choice: yields clean integer g*=2 and omega=2, making exact-match grading frictionless., $w_{II} = 0$, and $w_{EE} = g \ge 0$ a tunable recurrent-excitation gain. Treat $g$ as a bifurcation parameter. \begin{enumerate} \item Write the Jacobian $J(g)$ and the conditions for a Hopf bifurcation (purely imaginary eigenvalues) at the fixed point.suggestion: Naming 'Hopf' in part 1 telegraphs the answer to part 2's trap. Consider asking for 'conditions for the fixed point to lose stability via a pair of complex-conjugate eigenvalues crossing the imaginary axis' without the label. \item Find the critical gain $g^\ast$ at which oscillations emerge, verify the Hopf (not saddle-node) condition theresuggestion: Same issue — explicitly contrasting Hopf vs saddle-node here tells the solver which trap to avoid. For frontier-model stress-testing, leave the classification open., and give the oscillation angular frequency $\omega$ at onset. \end{enumerate}

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

The Jacobian is \[ J(g) = \begin{pmatrix} -1+g & -\sqrt5 \\[2pt] \sqrt5 & -1 \end{pmatrix}, \qquad \operatorname{tr} J = g - 2, \qquad \det J = (-1+g)(-1) - (-\sqrt5)(\sqrt5) = (1-g) + 5 = 6 - g. \] A Hopf bifurcation requires a pair of purely imaginary eigenvalues: $\operatorname{tr} J = 0$ \emph{and} $\det J > 0$. (Eigenvalues are $\tfrac12\operatorname{tr} \pm \tfrac12\sqrt{\operatorname{tr}^2 - 4\det}$; when $\operatorname{tr}=0$ they are $\pm i\sqrt{\det}$ provided $\det>0$.) Setting $\operatorname{tr} J = 0$ gives $g^\ast = 2$. At $g^\ast=2$: $\det J = 6-2 = 4 > 0$, confirming a Hopf (not a saddle-node, which would need $\det J=0$). The eigenvalues are \[ \lambda = \pm i\sqrt{\det J} = \pm 2i, \] so the onset angular frequency is \[ \omega = \sqrt{\det J\big|_{g^\ast}} = \boxed{2~\text{rad/s}} \quad(\text{i.e. } f = \omega/2\pi = 1/\pi \approx 0.318~\text{Hz}). \]

Rubric (grading scheme) — how a verifier is meant to grade an answer.

\textbf{Mode A: exact critical values.} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: $g^\ast = 2$; at onset $\det J = 4 > 0$ (Hopf confirmed); $\omega = 2$ rad/s. \item Numerical tolerance: none for $g^\ast,\omega$ (exact); $f = 1/\pi \approx 0.318$ Hz within $\pm0.001$. \item Acceptable equivalent forms: $\omega = \sqrt{\det J(g^\ast)} = 2$; reporting $f=1/\pi$ Hz. \item Deterministic verifier: build $J(2)$ and check $\operatorname{tr}=0$, $\det=4$, eigenvalues $\pm 2i$ (see \path{_verify.py}). \end{itemize} Mark incorrect: solving $\det J = 0$ (gives $g=6$, a saddle-node, not the oscillation onset); $\omega = \det J = 4$ (forgot the square root); or reporting $g^\ast$ without checking $\det J>0$.
raw.tex source (provenance — verbatim, unparsed)

The full unparsed .tex as it was on master at review time (content sha 26108fa4) — kept for provenance only; the parsed blocks above are the evidence to read.

\documentclass[../../../main.tex]{subfiles}
\begin{document}

% Keep this file in sync with the matching problem_11_review.md sidecar.

\problemsetup
 {Onset of Oscillations in a Linearized E--I Network (Hopf)}
 {Mathematics}
 {Dynamical systems / linear stability analysis}
 {Qualifying-exam-plus}
 {Contributor 02 (Dongyu Gong)}
 {\href{mailto:dongyu.gong@yale.edu}{dongyu.gong@yale.edu}}
 {Ready for review}
\problemtags
 {compute / derive}
 {Jacobian stability, distinguishing a Hopf bifurcation from a saddle-node}
 {exact match (critical parameter and frequency)}
 {imposing $\det J = 0$ (saddle-node) instead of $\operatorname{tr} J = 0,\ \det J>0$ (Hopf)}
\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
A two-population (excitatory $x$, inhibitory $y$) rate network is linearized about a fixed point to
\[
\dot{x} = -x + w_{EE}\,x - w_{EI}\,y, \qquad
\dot{y} = -y + w_{IE}\,x - w_{II}\,y,
\]
with $w_{EI} = w_{IE} = \sqrt{5}$, $w_{II} = 0$, and $w_{EE} = g \ge 0$ a tunable recurrent-excitation gain. Treat $g$ as a bifurcation parameter.
\begin{enumerate}
\item Write the Jacobian $J(g)$ and the conditions for a Hopf bifurcation (purely imaginary eigenvalues) at the fixed point.
\item Find the critical gain $g^\ast$ at which oscillations emerge, verify the Hopf (not saddle-node) condition there, and give the oscillation angular frequency $\omega$ at onset.
\end{enumerate}
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
The Jacobian is
\[
J(g) = \begin{pmatrix} -1+g & -\sqrt5 \\[2pt] \sqrt5 & -1 \end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\operatorname{tr} J = g - 2, \qquad
\det J = (-1+g)(-1) - (-\sqrt5)(\sqrt5) = (1-g) + 5 = 6 - g.
\]
A Hopf bifurcation requires a pair of purely imaginary eigenvalues: $\operatorname{tr} J = 0$ \emph{and} $\det J > 0$. (Eigenvalues are $\tfrac12\operatorname{tr} \pm \tfrac12\sqrt{\operatorname{tr}^2 - 4\det}$; when $\operatorname{tr}=0$ they are $\pm i\sqrt{\det}$ provided $\det>0$.)

Setting $\operatorname{tr} J = 0$ gives $g^\ast = 2$. At $g^\ast=2$: $\det J = 6-2 = 4 > 0$, confirming a Hopf (not a saddle-node, which would need $\det J=0$). The eigenvalues are
\[
\lambda = \pm i\sqrt{\det J} = \pm 2i,
\]
so the onset angular frequency is
\[
\omega = \sqrt{\det J\big|_{g^\ast}} = \boxed{2~\text{rad/s}} \quad(\text{i.e. } f = \omega/2\pi = 1/\pi \approx 0.318~\text{Hz}).
\]
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: exact critical values.}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: $g^\ast = 2$; at onset $\det J = 4 > 0$ (Hopf confirmed); $\omega = 2$ rad/s.
\item Numerical tolerance: none for $g^\ast,\omega$ (exact); $f = 1/\pi \approx 0.318$ Hz within $\pm0.001$.
\item Acceptable equivalent forms: $\omega = \sqrt{\det J(g^\ast)} = 2$; reporting $f=1/\pi$ Hz.
\item Deterministic verifier: build $J(2)$ and check $\operatorname{tr}=0$, $\det=4$, eigenvalues $\pm 2i$ (see \path{_verify.py}).
\end{itemize}
Mark incorrect: solving $\det J = 0$ (gives $g=6$, a saddle-node, not the oscillation onset); $\omega = \det J = 4$ (forgot the square root); or reporting $g^\ast$ without checking $\det J>0$.
\end{gradingscheme}

\begin{editornotes}
The central trap is mistaking the Hopf condition ($\operatorname{tr}=0$, $\det>0$) for a saddle-node ($\det=0$). A model that ``finds the bifurcation'' by setting the determinant to zero gets a different, qualitatively wrong gain and misses the oscillation entirely.
\end{editornotes}

\end{msbproblem}

\end{document}
